PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest

cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 2, 1892.
Wanted, an Australian Code.
J. C. Neild, M.L.A. of New South Wales, lias
recently arisen to carry on, in another direction,
the good work begun by the deceased Athenian
Solon some while since. Solon was the apostle
of cheap law ; J. C. Neild is the Mahomet of
low-priced lawyers. Solon had his laws cut
upon tablets of wood. Wood rots within 50
years at the outside ; the laws would hence, of
necessity, be re-cut twice a century. Solon
considered 50 years a good long life for any law.
He argued that people alter, that conditions
alter, and that the ideals of the present become
worm-eaten and time-gnawed within two
generations hence. The recutting of the laws,
therefore, on new-hewn wood, afforded future
legislators opportunity for revision and excision,
and, in short, re-editing Solon down to date.
J. C. Neild misses the point, and yearns for a
lawyer on a modern plan. He would leave the
machinery as it is and change only, the engineer.
This is almost as painfully illogical as an
attempt to feed a horse on beef-steaks, or an
anaconda on chaff and water.
* # #
Some days before the Christmas recess, Mr.
Neild lifted up his jawbone in the New
South Wales Parliament and demanded the
abolition of the regulations at present restricting
the practice of the law to the wealthy, who can
afford to devote several years to a life of semi-
idleness in alleged study at a university, where
nearly everything in the world is taught to the
budding lawyer, excepting a knowledge of the
law itself. Thereupon, all the lawyers in the
House rose as one man and protested strongly
against this encroachment upon their exclusive
and tradition-bastioned preserves. One light
of the Bar turned upbraidingly upon the
Labour party and demanded, petulantly:
“Where was their respect for trades-unionism?”
as if the business of the Labour party in the
House was to maintain the privileges of a legal
guild any more than those of a capitalistic ring.
Another lawyer compared the class to which he
belonged to the medical profession, and insisted
upon the necessity of preserving the public
from the exploitation of quacks. This, of
course, was an absolute giving away of the
whole case : for. where as quackery is the death
of medicine, there is no profession or trade
under the sun to which quackery is pure
life - blood and vital necessity more than
to the legal interest. But let us concede
that the lawyers and doctors stand on common
ground ; is it very romantic or Utopian then to
advocate a system of State-dispensed law and
State-dispensed medicine, in which system
lawyers and doctors would collect no fees from
the public, but, like the judge and the health-
officer, would be salaried by tim Government ?
We think not!
* * *
A third legal parliamentarian demanded not
easier but more difficult examinations and longer
periods of probation before the assumption of
the long robe and the horse-hair head-gear. He
pointed out that a knowledge of etymology was
of vast use in interpreting the terms of Acts in
archaic jargon, and that the large employment
of Latin in law made it necessary for intending
lawyers to devote huge sections of their lives
upon earth to the reading of Horace and Ovid
and the “ Satyricon ” of Petronius Arbiter.
Here, then, is a useful hint for providing the
public at the highest possible cost with another
legal functionary—the etymologist, whose busi-
ness shall be grammatical and explicatory, and
whose knowledge shall comprise not an ac-
quaintance with Justinian’s Institutes so much
as a comprehensive acquaintance with Max
Muller and Professor Sayce. In short, the
compiler of a good legal dictionary would be a
qualified Candidate for the office of Chief Jus-
tice, for the administration of the law is evi-
dently— taking our last-mentioned speaker for
guide—more a matter of the right (or wrong)
definition of a word or a phrase than the deter-
mination of a principle.
# # «
Ther e is, however, another side to the ques-
tion. Let us revert to Solon, and, instead of
complicating the mental machinery of the very
limited capacities of the average lawyer, en-
deavour to simplify the law. (Napoleon has
somewhat redeemed a reputation for wholesale
butchery by giving France a code. Let us boil
down the whole legal refuse at present in use
to something congruous, homogeneous, and more
suited to our needs. Instead of demanding
wide reading into the legislative barbarism of
the past from an expensive legal vampire, let
us amend, excise and codify all we require in
the way of law to our own present and vital
requirements. Why should not the various
provincial governments of Australia select some
of their ablest lawyers, and commit to them
the splendid task of framing a Federal Code ?
Let us abolish a condition of things which allows
an Australian government to revive age-dead
laws for the purpose of meeting exceptional con-
tingencies, and appeal to an enactment passed
in the reign of George IV. for the purpose
of suppressing a Queensland shearer. We
have men in Australia to whom we could
safely entrust such a work. In such a con-
nection the names of Chief Justice Higin-
botham, Julian Salomons, Q.C., and Samuel
Griffith, Q.C., will readily occur to hun-
dreds of Australians ; nor would a salary to
each of £5OOO per annum for five years be an
exorbitant expenditure for such a desirable
result. We are confronted with the alter-
native-cheap law or dear lawyers. Which
are we going to adopt ? Are we going to
bastion in the privileges of a monopolistic
guild, or are we going to rationalise our laws
and bring them into harmony with the con-
ditions of our existence ? J. C. JSTetld is out
of court—shall we adopt the method of Solon
and cut our enactments on the wood that
perishes ? In other words, do we mean to
periodically revise our laws, cast aside those
no longer of any value, and bring our code
right down to date. To commence this will be
one of the first privileges of Federated Aus-
tralia.
N.S.W. Civil Service Retrenchment.
The N.S. W. Civil Service has resolved to form
an association for defence against outside
enemies. In other words, it has resolved to
fall back on trades-union principles, but, for
the sake of the respectability that is embodied
in its black coat and its shiny hat, it spreads a
thin disguise over the fact. There is no pro-
vision for strike pay in its programme, for
going on strike is a common device only suited
to the common, horny toiler ; there are no
arrangements for boycotting anybody ; and
there is no real light of any kind in the whole
programme. Its objects are expressed as
follows :
(a) To establish unity throughout the service, whereby
its interests might be protected and advanced, (b) To
provide a representative organisation for the purpose of
thoroughly discussing all matters affecting the service,
and which shall he an acknowledged authority qualified
to express the voice thereof. (<•) To promote social
intercourse and encourage friendly feeling among mem-
bers of the service, and thus sweep away jealousies and
differences which will always prevail when men are dis-
united and have no rallying point, (d) To vindicate the
rights of the service and uphold its just claims ; to main-
tain respect for and confidence in the employes of the
State, to seek to obtain recognition of the principle that
there is no higher field of duty for a man than the faith-
ful service of his country, and that such service should
command due consideration and fair reward, (e) To
advocate the abolition of patronage and to uphold the
principle of advancement upon the grounds of seniority
and merit. (f) To advocate amendment of the present
Civil Service Act. (g) To provide a means of united
action in the event of the reasonable interests of the ser-
vice being endangered by sudden or extreme changes of
policy, (h) To collect statistics and other data in which
the service might be in any way interested, and to estab-
lish a library where all such information would be acces-
sible to members.
This is a suave and gentlemanly programme,
but if the Civil Service Association means to do
any real good, it had better resolve to drop
everything except Article E, and to fight for
Article E like a cart-load of wild cats. Patron-
age is the one huge grievance of the civil ser-
vant. The individual who has no political
influence remains a cheap and unconsidered
drudge in a Government office to the end of his
days. He contributes all his life to the
superannuation fund, and at the best only gets
an infinitesimal pension in his old age, and
generally gets none at all, but dies in a used-up
condition before lie is old enough to claim one.
Ho never, in any case, gets his pension before
he is fJU, while the man with influence often
gets Ids at 40. For the latter person is
often stricken with a mortal disease at that
age, and is retired on a. liberal annual allowance
out of the moneys paid by the miserable pariah
who has no relative in Parliament, and after
that he usually recovers and draws his allow-
ance for 50 years or thereabouts, and mean-
while the other man works for him and keeps
him going. Nobody ever enquires too closely
into the facts of the case, when a Civil-servant,
whose uncle is in Parliament, asks to be made
a genteel pauper in his middle-age, owing to
neurasthenia, or something ; but the man whose
uncle is not in Parliament would be stripped
and examined with a microscope before he
would get a cent out of the fund on such a
plea. The whole superannuation system is a
swindle, whereby politicians are enabled to
pension off their friends out of the earnings of
the country postmistress and the small girl
who drudges her life out in a bush school, and
similar unfortunates. It is a cold billet indeed
to be a Civil-servant without political influence.
Moreover, the Civil Service is the butt of
every Government that is hard-up, and every
newspaper that wants to gain a little cheap
popularity. There is a ceaseless demand for
“ retrenchment,” and retrenchment never falls
on the influential man with the lying medical
certificate and the pension, but always on the
drudge, who must stay on and be retrenched
because he has no other hopes or prospects.
The following extracts from a Sydney Sunday
paper are a fair sample of the war-whoop which
goes forth whenever New South Wales is hard-
up, and it is necessary to cut down somebody
else’s salary to meet the difficulty :
The annual revenue of the colony is, in round numbers.
£10,000,000, and the amount paid out of this for salaries
alone (exclusive of the military and naval vote of £228,043)
is £3,255,000, or equivalent to 32'5 per cent, of the whole.
In other words, one-third of the total revenue of New
South Wales is absorbed in the payment of salaries alone.
This assuredly is startling enough as placed in the light of
£ s. d., but it is still more so when a comparison is made
on the basis of population. The census of last March
gave the population of the colony us 1,132,501. The
number of persons in the employ of the Government is
as follows:
Permanent Officers ... ~ 24,709
Temporary Officers ~ .. 1,258
25,967
or one in every 48 of the entire population of the colony
—men, women, and children. Then, it we next turn to
Mr. Couiilan’s estimate of the male population (612,697),
and reasonably deduct one-half as being under working
age, it will be found that 1 in 11 of the adolescent and adult
male population is in receipt of Government pay. . . .
The population of Great Britain and Ireland is 38,609,000 ;
the revenue of the United Kingdom is £90,000,000 ; and
the Civil Service expenditure in salaries is £26,588,000—0r
29'5 per cent, of the entire revenue as againstN.S. W.’s32 - 5.
At first sight the difference (3 per cent.) does not appear
to be very great: but it must be remembered that the
British public, existing, as it does, under an extravagantly
costly monarchical form of Government, is saddled with
“ Civil List” burthens, for the equivalent of which we in
New South Wales have to make no provision. While
here, as already shown, one in 11 males of working age is
in receipt of Government pay, in Great Britain (array and
navy excluded) the proportion is about one in 65.
This sort of thing looks well for an incoherent
yell at election times, but it is awful drivel and
ignorance after all. In the first place, the
revenue of N.S. Wales is not £10,000,000, but
something less than £6,000,000. It is artificially
swelled on paper by putting down on the
revenue side a lot of such bogus items as “gross
railway receipts, £2,500,000,” and on the expen-
diture side such items as “ working expenses of
railways, £2,000,000,” instead of simply entering
as received the balance of half-a-million. This
sort of proceeding makes the revenue look
enormous, but the items are utterly meaning-
less, and the total is really nowhere above the
average. And the Civil Service is also made to
look something stupendous, as compared with
that of Great Britain. But then three-fourths
of it is made up of railway-servants, policemen,
and school teachers, none of whom come under
the Civil Service heading in England, and there
the whole comparison falls through. The al-
leged elevated Civil Service of N.S. Wales,
accepting the term as it is understood in Eng-
land and most other countries, numbers only
about 3400 individuals, or one in 300 of the
population, and most of them are paid at a rate
which leaves little room for retrenchment. It
ought, undoubtedly, to be cut down, but when
a reduction of the Service is advocated as a sure
means of making both ends meet and an alter-
native to Protection, it becomes a matter of
sheer insanity—there is so little of it that even
if the Civil Service, properly so called, was cut
down altogether, the saving would not fill the
gap. And the individuals who should be re-
trenched—the semi-idiot who has been shoved
into a profitable job by his uncle, the politician,
and the person who was pensioned off at 40 on
account of an incurable disease of which he
never seems to die—somehow never appear to
be affected by any scheme of retrenchment.
The Drivel of Our Fathers.
So far as can be judged by present appearances
the One-man-one-vote Bill will find its grave in
the New South Wales Legislative Council with
the politically smellful John Davies to officiate
as principal fossil at its interment. No definite
decision has yet been arrived at, but it is already
sufficiently evident that the sense of the nominee
House is—as usual —against any change in the old
order of things, and that the irresponsible
branch of the provincial Legislature is ranged, as
of old, on the side of Property, Up to date only
two members have damned the measure with faint
praise, and the rest have been uncompromisingly
hostile. The speeches, in almost every case, were
pitiable in their utter stupidity, and the efforts of
sundry councillors to offer a reason for their
opposition resulted in failure. A few doddering
old ruins plodded over the same weary arguments
which have been used and refuted over and over
again, but the majority simply stated that they
were “agin the Bill,” and intended to remain so,
and wisely went no further. The surpassing
idiocy of the New South Wales Constitution
appoints a Government to carry on the business
of the country and an Opposition to prevent them
doing it, and an Upper House to make dead sure
that they don’t do it, and the Upper House, in
the present instance, is fulfilling its purpose to the
letter. It exists for no earthly object except to
prevent the Government doing it, and its s”---'sss
in this respect is beyond all praise.
* * *
The Hon. John Davies, C.M.G., he of the
oily voice and the three old hats, struck a
sympathetic chord at the beginning of the
debate: —
Consider the question of oue-man-one-votc in another
aspect. Here is my honourable friend, Mr Vickery, whose
freehold property in Sydney is worth, perhaps, £500.000,
and who has goods stored in that property worth, per-
haps, £lOO,OOO. Is it just that the electoral' 'qualification
in respect of such property should he bestowed upon the
caretaker or the watchman, rather than upon the gentle-
man, who, through his industry and thrift, has acquired
it? Under the provisions of the Bill the hon. member
himself would receive no electoral qualification by reason
of his possessions in the city.
Now the thing of all others that glares out conspicu-
ously in the One-man-one-vote Bill is that no one
is to have a vote in respect of any property what-
ever, but solely because he is a sane, full-
grown, male Australian citizen out of gaol.
His address is registered, not because he is to
have a suffrage on account of having an address,
but simply in order that he can be found and
identified when he is wanted. The question
whether Davies’s gilded friend Vickery should
have an extra vote because his fortune is larger
than the average, and the other question whether
he should not also have another extra vote if Lis
ears are likewise larger than the average, also the
manifest impossibility of Vickery’s' pile or any
other of its colossal dimensions having been
accumulated by “industry and thrift,” and the
hideous improbability of Vickery being the
“friend ” of the apostle of the three hats, may all
be left, for the time being, out of the question.
The one utterly astonishing circumstance of the
case is that the Upper House can no more under-
stand the possibility of a pure manhood suffrage
than it can understand Chinese poetry, and
because Vickery is not to have an extra vote on
account of his property it concludes that that
extra vote is to be transferred to his watchman or
his caretaker or the outcast who camps on his
doorstep, and will, in all probability, reject the
measure on that account. It has been explained
several million times that under the new regime
there will be no vote whatever attached to pro-
perfey, but the Council is no nearer understanding
the point than it was at the beginning. The
matter might as well have been a Greek discourse
delivered to a deaf Hottentot. Either the Upper
House can’t read large print or else it is mentally
incapable of understanding it when it is read.
This gang of dotards might as wisely have objected
to the Bill on the ground that it would cause
cruelty to dogs, and prevent a deceased wife’s
sister being buried in consecrated ground; in
either case their incapacity could have gone no
further than it had already done.
» * • ,
An ancient person named Charles, however
who, by his own showing, had crawled from a
sick bed to block the cause of progress, broke even
this record. His reasons were as follows :
Who are the people it is proposed to disfranchise? My
memory carries me back 40 years—to the time when the
pioneers of settlement in this country went out into the
bush and purchased land which they cleared by their own
industry and hard labour. The money paid for the land
was applied to immigration— to bring out the men who
now cry for one-man-one-vote. Who really brought them
here? Why, the pioneers brought them here, made
homes for them, and spent years of industry clearing the
land and employing these men, who were brought out at
the public expense Is it just or reasonable
that these pioneers should be refused the right to vote for
representatives to make laws for the well-being of society
and the benefit of the country, and that the cowboys who
are on the farms should have a better right than the
owners who have improved the land ?
Probably it would be useless to tell this object of
public scorn that the meaning of one-man-one-vote
is simply that every man shall have a vote, and
that the “cowboy ” will not have a “ better right ”
than the landowner, but merely the same right-
no more and no less. And it would be
equally useless to point out that the Bill
docs not disfranchise any pioneer, for this
fossil is also incapable of comprehending plain
English. There is a wildness of mendacity in his
reasoning which is beyond all ordinary expression.
The assumption that every man who wants plural
voting was a pioneer, and that every man who
does not want plural voting was an assisted im-
migrant is altogether too large for words. More-
over, the pioneer obviously paid his money for the
land, and not to bring out discontented im-
migrants, and having got full value for the money
nobody owes him any debt of gratitude whatever.
Further, the cost of immigration mostly came out
of loan-votes; the boasted pioneer never “made
a home ” for anybody but himself ; and he did not
“ spend years of industry in employing those men
who were brought out at the public expense it
was the latter who silent their years working for
the alleged pioneer. ‘ The spectacle of that soulful
landowner being “refused the right to vote for
representatives to make laws fur the well-being of
society and the benefit of the country” is doubt-
less pathetic, but no one proposes or has proposed
to deprive him of the right, as Mr. Charles must
be aware. But all the same this picture of a
mythical pioneer deprived of his right to vote
visibly moved the House of ancient drivel, and
broughttheOne-Man-One-Vote Bill a shade nearer
to its final end.
* # #
Another mass of eloquence from the same
speaker is equally remarkable :
That is only one class of industrious men who are to be
deprived of their rights. There is another class of men
who came out and brought capital with them, or made
money here I)3' their industiy and went up the county,
took up stations and purchased homesteads. These men,
because they come to reside in Sydney to look after the
business connected with their stations, are to be deprived
of the right to vote whilst the rouseabouts on the stations
are to have it. It is simply ridiculous. In many instances
men have spent half a lifetime in Sydney in industry,
and invested their all in taking up land, clearing it and
stumping it, and yet these men are to be deprived of the
right to have a voice in making the laws of the country,
whereas the cowbo}' on the station is to have a vote.
The thing is infamous.
Possibly the unlettered wretch who gave way to
such awful rage over the disfranchised squatter
really believed that victim to be a genuine indivi-
dual, and failed to understand that his suffrage
was not going to be violently torn from him. An
Upper House and an aborigine are two things
whose credulity is almost beyond measurement.
# * #
The Council’s remaining reasons were, if pos-
sible, even a shade more imbecile. A legal
gentleman named Pilcher rose to point out that
the man who possessed no property should not be
allowed to vote at all, because he was in a posi-
tion to quit the country at any moment, whereas
the man of property was tied to the soil and
consequently had a “stake.” There was a horrible
incapacity about this logic, because the latter
individual can realise his property at any time,
and pay his way out of the country with the pro-
ceeds, while the man who has no property can’t
leave at all except on foot, and no one can quit
Australia on foot for obvious reasons ; therefore,
if Mr. Pilcher’s reasoning proved anything at
all, it proved that the capitalist should be dis-
franchised altogether, and no one save the dead-
beat be allowed to vote at all. But the gathering
of old cripples never even noticed the discrepancy.
One Hoskins asserted that the Ono-man-one-
vote principle had caused the failure of the last
South Australian loan, that it would lead to
mobs parading the streets at election times and
raising perdition generally ; and that only one
newspaper in all New South Wales was in
favour of the measure. Finally, Sir William
Manning approved of the One - man - one -
vote Bill in part, but proposed to move
an amendment in Committee whereby tho
plural vote of property should continue as at pre-
sent—in other words be demanded that every man
should have one vote, and some of them should
have more. And then, drearily and wretchedly,
the debate fizzled out. Excepting Mr. R. e!
O’Connor and bis namesake, Daniel, the mea-
sure apparently had not one single friend in the

the claims of property, and the claims of anything
else were never once mentioned. There was much
solemn declamation about “thrift,” but no other

cussion. Nobody talked of anything but
land and bricks and mortar. The whole pro-
ceeding was a dismal evidence that the
House was one-half unspeakable incapacity and
one-half bigotry and drivel. But unluckily the
Council is irremoveable, and its diseased brain
isn’t responsible to anybody. It is a gathering of
69 emperors, mostly insane, whose power, in a
passive sense, is utterly despotic. Practically

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Deadhead Subsidy.
Y Victorian Select Committee which recently
investigated the fraudulently abused system of
free railway-passes, has recommended, amongst
other things, that members of Parliament be no
longer pauperised with a general free pass all
over the province’s railways, but be conveyed
by train free only between their ordinary resi-
dences and the parliamentary capital. This
simplicity of recommendation has the small
fault of being a trifle too narrow in some places,
and much too broad in others. For instance, a
member of Parliament may be required to
travel elsewhere than to and from his ordinary
residence purely on the country’s business, in
which case he should be franked on the
country’s railways'. On the other hand, the
alleged ordinary, residence of an lion, member—
and° what would legally bo upheld to mean
his ordinary residence —may, in reality, be
his least - frequented residence. A member
for a country electorate who habitually
lives in town, but has a bark shanty away
towards the bounds of solitude, may neverthe-
less choose to regard the latter as his ordinary
residence, and, on the strength of his free pass,
may negotiate at any time, and as often as he
pleases, the free journey from the metro-
polis to any town between it and his shanty.
The mind gaily treasures the memory of a
cheerful Queensland legislator who lived in
Brisbane but owned a back-blocks station
several hundreds of miles away, and who reli-
giously charged his well-bled country mileage-
fees for the whole distance, though he never
travelled it. The remedy for the free-pass
evil seems to be that members should be allowed
free railway journeys only between Parliament
and their homes in actual use; and that, for every
other occasion on which a memberclaims to travel
on the country’s errand, he should make a
separate written application, setting forth the
reason of his journey. Under the present
system, some hon. members and their employers
simply loaf upon the IState, and, by favored
competition, shut their non-parliamentary busi-
ness rivals out of the market that would be
otherwise available for their skilled labour.
This will be move easily understood by a few
examples. Tiger Inglis, M.P., is a drummer
by profession, his “ line being tea. Those
who employ him—or if he employs himself, the
argument applies personally—do not require to
pay on his account a yearly sum for a general
railway ticket —£100, loss traders’ allowances.
So that the legislative drummer can afford to
go to work (we don’t say he does, mind
you) at a rate meaning £lOO per year less
than a non-parliamentary tea-vendor. Then yon
have Mr. Allen, M.P.—an insurance-canvasser.
How can non-parliamentary insurance men
hope to compete with a rival who can skip all
over the country as a railway deadhead ? Then
John Haynes, the newspaper man, can secure
a good deal of the “ inner running ” in respect
of country affairs that necessitate much railway
travelling. Would the Evening News, again,
have commissioned Mr. Black, M.P., if they
had been obliged to pay his railway fare—even
at reduced press rates—to Melbourne? In
short, is not the system an unfair subsidy that,
without reasonable justification, unduly handi-
caps the non-parliamentary workers whose
votes created these Ms.P. ?
Italian Immigration.
An American visitor, Mr. John Warren, of
Philadelphia, writes us an interesting—but un-
fortunately too lengthy—letter upon low-class
Italian immigration. The following fairly
demonstrates what that cheap European is in
the United States, and what he will yet be in
Bananaland, unless the natives arise and throw
him out ere it is too late :
The Germans, Swedes, Danes, and Irish—l am now
speaking of the nations which emigrate to America in
very large numbers—are our best colonists, for they come
to stay.' Most of them make good colonists themselves,
and the second and third generations of them, or their
blend with our native population, make our very best
citizens. To-day, on our railways, few Irishmen are
employed, and gangs of workmen along the lines are
nearly entirely Italians, employed in gangs, living
meanly in shanties near their work, doing their
own washing, what little is done, foraging the neigh-
bourhoods near by, spending none of their earnings
in the country from which they draw them, and
retiring after a few years to live, in contrast with the
beggary from which they came, like princes. Few, almost
none of them, seek citizenship, and those who do are
generally from among the merchants, fruit-vendors and
cooks. It has resulted, of course, that the wages have
gone down, and they now receive about 60 cents. (2s. (id.)
per day, or about one-half of what the Americans or
Irish-Aiuericans (who used to constitute the bulk of the
labourers) a few years since received. They are not even
known by their names, but generally by numbers. And
when it happens, as it does almost weekly, that one of
these unfortunates is cut down by a passing or backing
train (or not unfrequently by a comrade’s stiletto), the
intelligent, “coroner’s jury” return a verdict that “an
Italian, name to the inquest unknown, but called No. 49,
was accidentally killed, and we exonerate the
Railroad from any blame or fault in the premises.”
In our coal and iron mines, and iron and steel manu-
factories, and puddling furnaces and rolling mills, it
is even worse, for there the depraved Hun, the degraded
Pole, and the vicious Bohemian have displaced the Ameri-
can workman and driven him to tramp the roads and
sleep on benches in the parks and under the trees of the
forests. These wretches have as little notion of remain-
ing in the country as the low-grade Italians. They live,
if possible, more meanly, 15 or 20 of them often packed
■ike sardines, or undertakers’ coffins, on shelves in a room
12ft. by 14ft. in size. One woman often does the cooking,
washing, and other domestic services for the entire gang.
If goes without saying that they work for from one-half
to two-thirds the wages received by American working
men. That is the only reason of their being in the
country. So soon as they accumulate a thousand or two
dollars they and their money betake themselves hack to
their own country, and a new horde of vampires is sent
m er to suck more blood out of the American victim. We
nave absolutely prohibited the further entrance of the
Chinese, and our officials are vigilant to prevent their
being smuggled in. The siinple-natured Celestial, objec-
tionable as he is, is much belter in intelligence, industry,
morals, and habits than those Goths who are despoiling
American labour of its rich and hardly-earned heritage.
The one bright feature in this true picture is the
death of imported Italian No. 49. But even that
doesn’t amount to much, when Italians 1 to 48
are still alive, and a ship loaded down with more
degraded aliens Nos. 50 to 100 is always on the
way, to fill the gap left by the lost numeral’s
decease.
The Unconvicted Prisoner.
The brutality of the treatment of unconvicted
prisoners, all over Australia, is such that little
exception can be taken to our correspondent’s
observations :
Dear Bulletin, —ln re your remarks concerning Bell,
the now “pardoned” Melbourne man, who was falsely
imprisoned, and could not take steps to prove his
innocence, I wish to remark that it would be a good thing
if twenty or thirty of our law-makers could get into gaol
incog., for say six months each. It would soon bring
about reform in. the treatment of accused persons. Now,
ns things are, a man under arrest, and presumed by the
law to be innocent, is looked upon as an already-con-
victed criminal by the police, and the greatest injury he
can do a constable is to make a defence of any kind. I
have yet to meet the policeman who would not convict a
man he knew to be innocent, rather than own he
couldn’t find the real offender. As you say, when a
man is charged with an offence, he is shut off
from all communication with the world. The gaoler
insults him. He herds with drunks, thieves, and China-
men, the other prisoners crowd around and welcome him
as a fellow-thief and kindly ask him “ what he is in for.”
He lias to salute and cringe to a hulking bogman in
uniform, whose brogue you could cut with a knife, and
call him “ sir,” and if he refuses he is very likely thrashed
with handcuffs and locked in a cell, perhaps put into
irons. If he proves to be innocent there is nothing to
wipe out the stain, and for years after he is liable to have
a gaol-bird recognise him in the street and say before his
fiiends, “ Wot, don’t yer know me ? W’y, we was in quod
together,” Once I was reporting a case where a young
man was charged with stealing a watch from a drunken
commercial traveller. The young fellow had a few
pounds which were wolfed up by a solicitor in appearing
at five remands, waiting for the prosecutor. When the
youth was finally committed for trial there was no money
left to fee a banister, and the solicitor abandoned him. I
took the affair up out of sheer pity, and at once found
that during the time the supposed robbery was com-
mitted, the prisoner had been “ on the spree ” at a hotel
twenty miles away and had never been in the town at all,
although the prosecutor swore they went for a drive
together. On the day of the trial I brought the landlady to
court, and when the sergeant heard she was a witness for the
defence his rage was boundless. He said: “ How dar 3 r e
intherfere wid this case? Oi blave ye’re in it yerself, and
for two pins Oi'd lock ye up.” He went to the witnesses
and tried to bully them into owning that they were paid
to prove an alibi, but they were square people, and the
prisoner was acquitted. I made a bitter enemy of the
sergeant, who never forgave me, although it was after-
wards discovered that the traveller had sold the watch
while drunk. This prisoner was six weeks in gaol and
had a maddening experience. Shut up in a Cell at 5 p.m.
daily, without light or books, he passed nights of sleep-
- less' agony, with nothing but a blanket and mat between
his bones and the cement floor, and caught a cold which
has injured him for life. Up at five in the morning, he,
a i uuconvieted and innocent man, had to perform dis-
gusting offices and to line up in the gaol yard with the
semirings of the streets ; then he shivered in the cold till
8, when he breakfasted on a dish of hominy, and was
indebted to some man “doing time” for a spoonful of
sugar to sweeten the loathsome mess. He had no books
and no companionship. He could write a letter or tele-
gram, but the gaoler would not send cither for delivery,
as he said “ VYe don’t keep messengers to wait on s
like you.” Worse than all, he had to salute this ruffian
and call him “ Sir,” which was the bitterest pill of
all. “ Brice Waning ” hits the nail on the head when he
says the old convict warder loved to see a gentleman
salute him, and his successor loves it to this day. When
a certain distinguished man was in gaol these little things
were doubtless softened for him, but I think, in memory
of the time he spent in Darlinghurst, he ought to do
something to ameliorate the condition of prisoners await-
ing trial.— Reform.
Consols and Confiscation.
One of the principal troubles of Australia at
the present moment is that it can’t raise any
more loan-money in England, except with great
difficulty and at exorbitant interest. England,
on the other hand, can raise money whenever it
wants it, and its securities are in such an
exuberant condition that it has reduced the
interest on Consols from the miserable rate of
three per cent, to the supremely wretched one
of 2|, without any visible injury to the public
credit. These facts are quoted until the whole
world gets tired, to prove (1) that Australia,
chiefly through Protection, and the formation
of a great Labour movement, and the conse-
quent “insecurity of Property,” is no longer
regarded as offering good enough security to the
British loan-monger ; and (2) that Hingland,
through Freetrade and a strict adherence to
“sound constitutional principles,” is the
grandest and most solvent country on earth.
Both these statements are rampant folly. Aus-
tralia can’t get money because England, in its
general decadence under the Freetrade system,
has no longer any loose cash to lend, because it
has fooled away what spare change it had in
loans at Hebrew interest to wild-cat States
which can’t pay, and because almost all its big
private hanks are on the verge of ruin, and even
the Bank of England is no longer what it seems.
And England can get money still, even at 2|
per cent., because Consols are as truly a forced
and irredeemable currency as the depreciated
paper-money of Russia, or the hopeless bonds
of Argentina, and their price is kept up by
brute-force and nothing else. In England the
investment of trust funds is limited by the
Court of Equity to Consols, Bank of England
stock, and Indian stock. Everybody who holds
money in trust, unless otherwise directed by
the will under which it is held, must put it in
one of these three securities. The only people
who buy Consols are trustees, who do it from
necessity, and if the interest were 2 per cent.,
or 1 per cent., or nothing at all per cent., they
must buy them all the same. There are hardly
enough Consols to go round, in fact, and there-
fore there is a forced competition for the miser-
able British waste-paper, which keeps the price
up to an exorbitant figure. If this law were
repealed, the British 2per cent, securities
would drop like a stone, and if Britain wanted
to borrow, it would probably have to pay 4 per
cent, or go without. And if a similar law were
enacted m Australia there would either be a
revolution or else Australia would he able to
borrow locally all the money it wanted at any
interest it liked to offer.
Mrs. Scarcebkook, of Melbourne, found “not
guilty ” (on the ground of insanity) of having?
while suffering from puerperal fever, murdered
her sou, has recovered her'senses and lias been
discharged from gaol, where she was imprisoned
“ during H.M.’s pleasure.” Up to date, the
invariable practice has hc'hn to remorselessly
keep in confinement for life people who had
committed homicide while suffering from in-
sanity—whether temporary or permanent —the
official idea having, no doubt, been that they
were sure in any case to get mad in the long
run. The Bulletin regards the release of Mrs.
Scarcebrook as an indirect victory for Dr.
Mullen and his colleagues, whose equitable
and common-sense contention that release
should depend not on anyone’s caprice, but
merely on the production to the Supreme Court
of evidence that the prisoner has become sane,
will yet be the law of the land.
Some of the Canadian politicians now say that
the annexation question — i. e. , political fusion
of Canada with the United States, involves a
civil war. The Bulletin doesn’t believe there
will be any war. It believes that Canada will
run off with the United States in the same
natural manner as a woman elopes with another
woman’s husband. But civil war, anyhow, is
the least abominable kind ot military strife,
inasmuch as it must be directed, more or less,
by popular impulses, and express the rival wills
of two strong sections of one people. The
theory that there is something especially
horrible in battles where blood-relations may
chance to fight on opposite sides is merely
pulpit cant. Civil war, by localising human
suffering, tends to keep it limited, as
compared with the woe that results from,
say, a Franco - German slaughter, because
if a son shoots his father when his father
happens to be shooting him, they leave
only one weeping widow and mother between
them, whereas for every brace of unrelated
corpses there must be two separate lots of
family bereavements. Also the corpses in the
one instance have the satisfaction of knowing
that they died at their own voluntary risk for
a principle they knew something of, whilst in
the other case they peg out with a miserable
conviction that they didn’t want to fight from
the beginning, and, by Jingo ! if they did, then
they were ignorant of any reason for wanting
to. These last remarks apply only to common
private soldiers who make “military glory,”
not to the men who give the order and scoop all
the glory after it is made.

Nepotism is spreading in the New South Wales
public service, a regulation to the contrary not-
withstanding. In department after department
one hears of the permanent head being the
superior officer of his own son, or nephew, or
brother. How pleasant for old officers to find
themselves working side by side with the chief’s
son, fresh from school, perhaps, and prone to
tale-bearing and mischief-making ! Of course,
every chief’s sou will not stoop to repeat the
conversation of his comrades to his father : but
it is done in at least several departments. The
spread of nepotism should be stayed, or else the
whole service will he fractionised and its
efficiency impaired. In the Custom House, one
finds a son of the Collector of Customs and a
son of another Customs official. In the Parlia-
mentary departments it is the same. One hears
of a son of the Clerk of the Assembly being
billeted in the Assembly department, and a
nephew of a Speaker appointed almost in suc-
cession to the nephew of a previous Speaker.
In the Council department, a father and son are
employed side by side ; while into the Hansard
department the IT nder - Secretary for Shorthand
has introduced a son with no previous press
experience. How many of these favoured indi-
viduals have passed the Civil Service examina-
tion ?
London Graphic records that a man
named Jobling, hanged in England for killing a
magistrate, was gibbeted (at Durham) as recently
as 1832. The body was escorted to Jarrow Stake,
stripped, covered with pitch, and reclothed. It
was then carefully encased in a framework of iron,
the face being wrapped in a white cloth, and hung
on a gibbet 21 ft. high, and bound with iron
bands. Jobling’s widow has just died at the great
age of ninety-six, and so the last personal link
with the gibbet has been severed.
More trouble. The Bulletin fixed up a
harmless and gentlemanly paragraph, setting forth
that a well-known lawyer was going to resume
“ criminal practice,” and the compositor at once
girded himself up for the fray, and made it read
that he was going to resume “criminal practices.”
Now obviously, if a man “resumes” a thing, it
follows that he has been at it before, and the
ghastly impossibility of the lawyer in question
having ever been in any criminal practices is too
conspicuous to need further comment.
Graham Berry, addressing a mob of kin-
dred souls at one of his farewell banquets, said
“ He felt he had been greatly improved by the
valuable experience he had gained in the position
of Agent-General for Victoria. He was willing
to admit that he was sorry to leave London.”
After which the elderly renegade proceeded, with
a salt tear in his bold, blue eye, to explain that
Premier Munro had offered him a renewal of his
lease of office, but had subsequently withdrawn
the offer, and burst up Berry’s dream of further
improvement and more larks in London, without
a word of apology. Decidedly rough on rats !
Good Graham, we offer our pity,
For pity costs naught to bestow —
From your nest in New Babylon city
You’ve got Jimmy’s order to go ;
So snug was the berth you are leaving—
Light work and such excellent pay—
That we freely believe you are grieving
To find you must flutter away.
You had fairly set up as a Tory,
With a handle in front of i our name ;
You have raved of Himperial glory,
And played the Himperial game
In stockings of silk and knee-breeches.
Does it strike you as vulgarly odd
That you once made Republican speeches
And posed as a democrat god ?
0! no doubt you’ve been slowly “improving”
Through all these respectable years ;
We have twigged that the spirit was moving
Your tongue to beslaver the peers,
Whilst the echo grew fainter and fainter
Of words that you thundered of yore,
When vou talked about “ cutting the painter,”
But dreamed of a knighthood in store.
You are grieved when you think of returning,
For you know that our masses will snort
At the brand who was plucked from the “burning,”
And anon was presented at Court;
You must meet them a month or two later,
And had hotter prepare to he told
That the knight of to-day is a traitor
To the men who “ made ” Berry of old.
Musings in Maoriland.*
Under the above title, Mr. Thomas Bracken, in a
portly tome of 360 pages, presents the world with
his poetical offspring, introduced and preceded by
an historical sketch of Maoriland by Sir Robert
Stout, and a preface by Sir George Grey. These
prose contributions, wo are sorry to note, are all
very well in their way, but a volume of poems is
not a history, and the preface is not wanted
good wine needs no bush, and a man’s verst!
cannot gain in quality by anybody's personal
recommendation. The volume is legibly printed
on good paper, but the typographical ornamenta-
tion is painfully German ; and the pictures, to
say the least of them, might have been consider-
ably more artistic. There is much gilt on the
covers—in fact too much ; but all this has little or
nothing to do with the poems themselves, which
constitute a body of very respectable verse, and—
in a few instances—of very fine verse. In this
collection is printed that noble and deeply philo-
sophical poem, “ Not Understood,” which has
already appeared in The Bulletin, and which,
though well-known, is particularly worth quoting
here :
NOT UNDERSTOOD.
Not understood. We move along asunder,
Our paths trow wider as the seasons creep
Along lire years ; we marvel and wo wonder
Why life is life? And then we fall asleep,
Not understood.
Not understood. We gather false impressions,.
And hug them closer as the years go by,
Till virtues often seem to us transgressions ;
And thus men rise and fall, and live and die,
Not understood.
Not understood. Poor souls with stunted vision
Oft measure giants by their narrow gauge ;
The poisoned shafts of falsehood and derision
Are oft impelled ’gainst those who mould the 0;.,e
Not understood.
Not understood. The secret springs of action,
Which lie beneath the smface of t he show.
Are disregarded ; with self-satisfaction,
We judge our neighbours, and they often go,
Not understood.
Not understood. How trifles often change ns :
The thoughtless sentence or the fancied slight
Destroy long years of friendship and estrange n ,
And on our souls there falls a freezing blight.
Not understood.
Not understood. How many breasts are aching
For lack of sympathy 1 Ah ! day by day,
How many cheerless, lonely hearts are breaking?
How many noble spirits pass away
Not understood?
Oh, God ! that men would see a little clearer,
Or judge less harshly where they cannot see ;
Oh, God ! that men would draw a little nearer
To one another, they’d he nearer Thee,
And understood.
# # *
Mr. Bracken's volume contains dozens of sweet
little lyrics, such as “ Pax Vobiscum ”; in fact,
the poet is, in our opinion, at his best just in
these. His more ambitious verses are somewhat
stilted, and his lays of old days—as “Old Ben-
digo,” for instance—rather bald and forced. The
short poem, “He Profund is,” is full of noble
sentiment, but we would Mr. Bracken had em-
ployed a more serious metre. Some of his longer
pieces breathe a strong martial feeling, and a
reciter anxious to score a triumph with something
new might do much worse than essay the delivery
of “ How Yon Tempsky Died,” and if the said
reciter desired a tale to please a woman’s fancy
“ The Christmas Queen,” a romantic story rhymed
in graceful verse, is ready to his hand. In short ,
Mr. Bracken is chiefly strong in versified narra-
tive, and one short piece like “ Leah ” is worth all
his long efforts like “Jubilee Day.” “Sturt’s
Last Letter” is an ambitious poem, but the
guerdon of the dying explorer’s hope—a knight-
hood —is powerless to move the reader’s sym-
pathy. One can only feel sorry that a great man
could, at the same time, have been so small.
Amongst the versified stories we cannot give the
“ Old Log Hut ”so high a place as “ The Christ-
mas Queen,” for it falls short in artistic treatment,
but it is still very good. Amongst the “serious”
and patriotic poems we confess a weakness for the
little poem to Sir George Grey, in which the great
Pro-Consul is likened unto a grand old tree,
towering high above all other forest-growths, and
giving harbourage and shelter to innumerable
songsters of the grove.
* # #
Undoubtedly Mr. Bracken is, as a poet, far
more successful in versified narrative, and in
philosophical and contemplative verse like “Not
Understood ” and the “ Soul’s Treasury,” than in
historic poems of the “ Jubilee ” character, or in
descriptive poems touching the characteristics
of Maoriland landscape Sir George Grey’s
opinion to the contrary notwithstanding. By the
way, while at this present moment the Anglo-
Saxon world and Mr. Stead are raving concerning
the rights and privileges of Eve’s daughters, Mr.
Bracken in a few verses, yclept “Woman’s
Rights,” reviews the situation from man’s more
romantic point of view—and we should recommend
the shrieking sisterhood and their shriekier
brothers to peruse them. Our Maoriland poet
turns time and again to social questions, and
“Only a Miserable Wretch,” “The Beggar’s
Prayer,” and other pieces, show how much he is
haunted by the evils wrought by man on men. We
cannot admit a special fondness for the “March
of Te Rauparaha; ”an English poem composed of
words from a foreign language. —vide Longfellow’s
“ Hiawatha ” —is unrelishable by the Anglo-Saxon
outsider, and is best summed up in little Martin
Farquhar Topper’s Orphic deliverance anent bon
bon mottoes, “ it may be exceeding clever, but—
ivc cannot understand it.” In conclusion, we
recommend the reader of this short notice to read
Mr. Bracken for himself. The time could be much
less profitably spent, and bis volume is so large
and our space is so limited that we can do little
more than run over the names of a few of his best
efforts.

Mudgee Independent, has coolly appro-

the Freeman and palmed them off as its own -
as it is now doing with “ Ben Duggan.”

My Dkah Mooradinua, —
The whispered Sydney scandal of the moment affects
a, well-known professional man of literary instincts who is,
a hopeless grass-widower—and a lady, of course. Now, he
and she fell in love—and many were the blissful meetings
at an arranged rendezvous, and things were as cuddle-
a.ome as could be until one evening Romeo turned up at
the tryst, but waited, in vain for the Jijliet who never
came. A night of agonised apprehension (please grasp
the fact that he was madly in love with the lady) was
capped in the morning by a letter from her, stating in
curt terms that as she couldn’t marry him she had decided
tp marry somebody else, and had done so the preceding
afternoon. The curtain drops here on a blighted soul,
but rises to discover all the parties at the theatre—the
happy bride and groom sparkling in the front row and
Romeo hovering in the gloomy background and toying
with a shiny revolver. But just as he was about to shoot
a friend who was with him seized his hand in horror and
dragged him away in time to rob the daily papers of the
biggest kind of sassiety sensation, and Romeo buried his
revenge and went home. In scene four all parties are
again at the theatre—Romeo (alone), and Juliet and a
lady friend. Somebody (a man) comes and whispers to
Romeo ; he returns a decisive “ no”; another male Mercury
prattles in his ear—he again refuses ; the third messenger
taps him lightly on the shoulder with a gloved hand—it
is Juliet herself, and he rises, coldly polite, and follows
her. Then in the deserted cloak-room there is a scene
that wants a sassiety novelist to do it justice—a lovely
being in full dress crouching at the feet of the man who
still loves her, confessing her marriage was a mad freak,
and that “you—you only,” &c., and suddenly tearing off
her wedding-ring and swearing to follow him to the end
of the world ! But the flabbergasted Romeo (perhaps he
remembered in time the feather-weight of her former
vows and tears) calmly picked up the ring and returned
it to her, kissing the back of her hand as he did so—and
went out and left her without a word. Beafttifisly
romantic, isn’t it? But I would like to edge in an
expression of deep and honest sympathy for Mr. Juliet.
That matrimony is a very risky operation the divorce-
court abundantly proves. A man was matrimonially un-
chained in Sydney recently, and compelled to pay two
guineas a week aliniohy to his wife. He re-married (you
can’t scare some people), and on his representing to the
court that he was a beggar and a vagrant on the face of
the earth, the alimony was reduced to one pound per
week. I should think that man must hav'e a very fair
idea of what the pecuniary sensations of the Mormon are,
shouldn’t you? and the dreary situation suggests the
query, “ Has a divorced man to pay alimony to his ex-
wife when she re-marries?” I expect he has. The law
always punishes a man who makes a mistake in marry-
ing. Nobody ever hears; of a giddy divorced wife
having to soothe a deceived husband’s feelings at
the rate of thirty. Bishop Smiths a week! she can
wreck his 'appy ’ome apparently and riot among tire ruilis
without being sixpence out of pocket, hut if he plays
tricks with the domestic bliss, the injured dove goes
home to her ma, and he has still to provide the where-
withal to cover its keep. There seems to be no nasty
pride about these unwived wives—they wouldn’t walk on
the same side of the street as the Horrid Brute, of course,
but they take his money all the same. And -the ques-
tion again—if these ill-used angels annex another hus-
band, has the ex-spouse to still keep up his legal
contribution towards the expenses of the new “ men-
agerie? In short, is divorced-wife’s-alimony a grinding,
remorseless tax that only ceases when the undertaker
takes one or other of the parties for a drive ?
Considering that each stuccoed Sydney mansion has
every blind down to notify the owners’ fashionable with-'
drawal into the back rooms or the coal-cellar until the
recuperating season wanes, it isn’t surprising to again
record a disheartening paucity of beauty and fashion at
the last day at Tattersall’s meeting at Randwick. There-
fore I can shriek aloud over nobody’s clothes this issue—
except, perhaps, the draught-board-checked covering of a
fat lady which caused one to abstractedly murmur, “My
move, I believe,” and a brace or two of women with
chalked boots on. Now, I’m not going to gnash my teeth
and foam at the pen over these pipe-clayed hoof-cases.
They’re every bit as magnifying as tan boots, hut
I only mean to protest against, the wholesale way
most of the wearers pitted them against thdir
toilets. Nobody . could quarrel with white boots
fringing a white frock, but when they’re the only white
spot about you, your pedestals seem to rise up and hurl
themselves at the company. One girl in a pink turn-out
finished off with snowy boots reminded me irresistibly of
Galatea before the vivifying process had got to her ankles.
There was no vice-regal pivot for the proceedings to
revolve around, and when the band tooted a bar or so of
“G.S.T.Q.” only two hats were raised in tribute to the
melody.
What “proud-sturamicked” boarders have to put up
with at table during the “ festive ” season is never fully
appreciated by the home-living portion of society. Then
the rich uucouthness' from beyond the skyline swarms
upon the hoarding-house and introduces new manners
that ought to he specially interdicted by printed notices
in the hall. And the tricks of the artificially.physically-
repaired tribe snatch the laurels from the vulgarian who is
as nature arranged him, and who can’t horrify the company
by meditatively taking out a front tooth to get at a fish-
bone, or absent-mindedly remove a natural-looking glass
eye and play marbles with it among the victuals. A
Sydney hash-house had a bad experience on Christmas
Day. The culprit was from the cattle districts and had a
set of brand-new teeth fixe. 1 in for Christmas, and when
be had finished feeding he gapingly unhitched the gold
plate from his upper-jaw and dropped it with' a sigh of
relief into his tumbler of water. Nobody waited to hear
him gummily explain that the dentist had said to be par-
ticular to wash them after special ’ard work.
Women who live in boarding-houses and wear artificial
teeth should curb their passion tor dipping their beaks
"'to scalding-hot beverages, if they want to keep the
fa ity of.their long, yellow fangs as dead a secret as is
possible in a matter-of-fact age where even the adoring
lover, before he commits himself, likes to get his Dulcinea
gazing moon-wards with her mouth open just to see
whether her grinders, are all in situ. But I digress, At
a very swell Potts Point table d’hote the other day, as
Miss Blank (who takes her gargle direct from the flaming
cauldron) let her first boiling sip spread-eagle over her
palate, brittle, cracking sounds were heard in her jaws,
and, lo and behold, every composite tooth in her gold-
harnessed gums was split from root to tip ! I am told
that piping-hot whisky cracks the masquerading masti-
cators quickest of anything, so you should always lock
the cellaret before audiencing any lady whose original
five-guinea set, of, twenty-four is evenly divided into a
toothy fringe of,forty-eight.
Numbers of parents of wooden-headed offspring—who
when asked what they would like to be when they grow
up a big man, respond with a lack-lustre “ dunno ’’—take
their hopeless olive-branches to the bump-prophet o i 1
and over again to find out what sonny is best at, when
sonny ever fails to evolve a future hope except to become
a “bushranger.” The skull scientists must get fairly
tired at these battalions of low-browed brats coming to
have their hollow heads subdivided, and one professor
let his feelings run away with him to a proud papa—a
well-known Sydney solicitor—who wanted him to plumb
the intellectual depths of his son and heir. “ I think
you’ll find the makings of a smart lawyer here, Bumps,”
said the fond father, pushing Billy forward. But after
feeling pause the brain-reader replied: “His bump . f
amaliveness is the only noticeable development. You
might make him a model Mormon.”
A wealthy Australian woman, whose grown-up, sons all
have had a try for the professions, but who have all failed
utterly, and betaken themselves to beet and mutton
raising, has one untried hopeful left whom Nature has
fitted with a cranium the size of a prize water-melon.
On this outward and visible evidence of water-on-the-
brain, the proud mamma pins hopes that Johnny will
one day be a doctor—“so nice to have a doctor in the
family, you know”—and at afternoon-tea at, a friend’s
house recently, she jocularly tapped Johnny’s swelled
head-piece, saying, “ I'm going to take him to a phrenq
legist—there must be 'something inside all this, and you
shall hear just what the man says.” But the sad part of
it all is that, though Johnny’s culminating bulge was
carefully mapped out by the bump-feeler, the mamma
has never yet divulged what he found there. Another
sheep and cattle man, probably.

New Year’s Day in the presence of a chaotic sea of,

These last looked very groggy in the cruel daylight'

They rehearsed everything very carefully for the
“ Messiah “oratorio given at the Tasmanian Exhibition,
on Xmas night, l am told, but what they evidently didn’t
rehearse was the appearance of a baritone singer of a
decidedly Oriental cast of countenance standing in the
front row of the lady chorus and surrounded by lovely
Tasmaniehnes, while the rest of the male singers were in
the misty background miles away from the fun. Under
these Mormonish circumstances it seemed only right to
have the stage draped with the Turkish ensign, since the
baritone heading the beauteous bevy was about as faithful
a reproduction of the Grand Turk and his harem out for
a walk as you could hope for in this outwardly mono-
gamous country.
Somewhere in the Banana region they got up a tourna-
ment, the other day, in which each knight was nominated
by a lad ye, who shrodded her identity under a nom.-de-
theatre (as the women did in ancient times, calling
themselves “Ye Guelder Rose,” and so on). But the
Queensland gal—raised on damper and billy-tea—has a
fine scorn for “ poitry ” when applied to herself, and with
unconscious homage to the eternal fitness of things, wrote
herself down as “Ironbark Emma,” “Ti-tree Tilly”
“Sarsaparilla Sal,” &c.—a long, patriotic list, but with
one exception, She was a long, raw-boned love, hollowed
out wherever she shouldn’t have been, with a camel-like
profile, and loqsely-lixed tomato hair, combined with fan-
like feet and an explosive stutter, and she soared above
the ruck. Her knight scoured round the arena under
the segis of “ Queen of the May.”
A Consul in Australia for a far-away Republic enlisted
in the cavalry of his particular “ kawliny ” on the express
condition that if his nation declared war against An,, i.e
lia he wouldn’t have to fight. There’s about as much
•chance of his country rushing belligerently upon us as
there is that a cat will go for a swim, but the patriotic
Consul hung back until assured that he wouldn’t have to
carve his nearest relatives in the interests of an alien
land, and in the end enlisted in peace. It behoves every
representative of red tape to make a tremendous official
fuss whenever the opportunity presents itself, dontchew-
know.

They are keen traders in Monaro, or
Groagingolong, or somewhere. The squatter
assassinates the dingo and takes his hide to a
shire councillor in Victoria, who is officially
authorised to buy, and gets ,£1 reward. Then
for a consideration of ss. he re-purchases the
scalp and tail and_ trades the relics for =£l in
N.S.W., thus clearing 355. out of one dingo.

One of his remarks is well worth q.uotina - . “ The

Londonderry looked remarkably well in a sable-
trimmpd pelisse, which cost .£500.” At a London
dead-house, the same week, Mary Crae looked
remarkably well on, the slab, fashionably attired
“in one thm, wretched garment. She had died
of pneumonia, the result of starvation. Her
twelve-weeks-old child; had died at her breast five
weeks before, and been buried by the parish.
The mother’s body was entirely destitute of fat.
John Crae, her husband, died in hospital in
October through an injury received while at work.
It appeared that the woman had sold her only
pair of boots to buy a milk-bottle for the child.”
The evening edition ofj the &M. Herald
departs from the last glimmer of the shore-light
of truth in insinuating that the retrenchment
party in N.S.W. Assembly contemplate a whole-
sale sacking—that is to say, a cataclysm, in which
the worthy and the unworthy alike will be in-
volved, a policy of indiscriminate dismissal. The
Echo declares that the N.S.W. Protectionist
party is. composed of men professing the same
radical views vyhiph prevailed in Victoria shortly
after the Protectionists gained a majority, and
carefully proceeds to prophesy what, in its childish
ignorance of Australian history, it calls “Black
Monday,” Now, as the world knows, a Pro-
tective policy had nothing whatever to do
with . Black Wednesday. Protection has pre-
vailed; in Victoria from 1860 to the present
hour. It was initiated by McCulloch, and
Black Wednesday occurred about 1878, under
Berry’s premiership, and it was brought about
not by Protection—for that was a familiar and
fully domiciled creature-but by the haughty
refusal of the Legislative Council to pass a land-
tax. A deadlock occurred, and Berry, disdaining
to avail himself of the tactics pursued by
M'Culloch during the first deadlock, intimated
that, being unable to pay, the Government must
retrench, anti accordingly dismissed every civil
servant outside the Supreme Court Bench and the
Penal and Police services who could be dispensed
witffi By.this Berry brought the Conservative—
that is to say, the Ereetrade—party to its knees.
Beyond their ordinary concern as gropers in the
quagmire of fiscal discussion, the N.S.W. civil
servants have no more interest in the domination
of either party than have the rest of the com-
munity. It was Sir Henry Parkes who used con-
temptuous language about the “ bitter bread of the
Civil Service.” 'The attempt to set the service
against the Dibbs Government by the wolf cry of
a Black Wednesday is illustrative of the lengths
to which the baffled and defeated Calico Jemmy
party will proceed.

OUT OF TOWN.
In vain the unpaid butcher calls ;
The mansion’s blinds are fastened down,
A passing- cabman loudly bawls—
“ Them folks is out of town.”
The milkman, with his long, long bill,
Loiters around the garden’s fountains,
Tid someone through the gates calls shril’,
“The’ve bin a fortnit up the nrmntains,”
The baker-man falls due in vain—
lie cannot And a soul who’ll pay,
So he climbs to his seat again—
The Swindell-Grabbs have gone away
But one dread day grim “ wood and coal,”
Who thought to modify disaster,
Up to the silent backyard stole,
To shake some coke back for his master.
To force the door he picked the lock—
What! Lights? A gruff voice, “ IIV.7, young fellar!
The coal-man staggered at the shock :
The Swindell-Grabbs were in the cellar.

So far as concerns the fire and the
apparitions and the general unaccountableness,
'- lle orthodox pantomime and the pantomime
at Sydney Royal is decidedly orthodox —is
precisely the same thing as opium; tiie present
writer has tried them both, and to this day
he doesn’t know with any certainty which is
opium and which is pantomime. r i’he “Forty
thieves” is filled to the brim with that howling
confusion which is the one great essential in all
such entertainments. It is a misguided ambition
in red paint—an insane panorama in a state of
insurrection—a rush of forty thousand kaleido-
scopes down the gable of a rainbow into
a sea of limelight and baleful green fire.
It is the sort of show which makes one’s
brain dissolve when he looks at it, and rush
foaming down steep precipices in Ids head into
diep, cold pools of disorganised think matter at
t.ie bottom. And withal there is understood
to be a plot in it, but what that plot is no
one shall ever know, for it is more disjointed
•i U | l ra °k e d heretic or a cryptogram torn by
wild horses. It contains shreds an ! specimens if,
everything under the sun, and many other things,
besides; there are dervishes in it,' and mangles-,
and thieves, girls in skirts, girls in huffy things,
that are undefinable, niggers, cockneys, giants,,
dwarfs, Arabs, washerwomen, donkeys, princes,
monarchs that are more or less intoxicated, frantic.
acrobats who swallow themselves and
and then spit themselves out again, and shin up-
nothing into the empty air; bulls, demons, yells,,
armour, bailiffs, princesses, sweet music, groans!,
devils, and a maddening whiz of things in general!.
1 here are any number of good actors in it also*,
but towards the end they are all lost in the general,
not, and no one can tell one from the other; and
there is much lovely scenery, but the mountains
begin to fight in the final delirium, and the trees
rush after each other, and the temples elope and
get tight. And, finally, there is oblivion, and
the writer of this notice slowly recovered his
senses on the pavement for he was giddy and had
pantomime in the head ; and the one thing which
remained distinct in his tattered memory was
that, at some part ov other of the performance,
somebody in the show had hollered “God save
the Queen,” and it had fallen flat. But who
hollered it, or what caused him to holler, is a for-
gotten, circumstance and will remain so unto
seventy times seven.

Fui’ther consideration of “La Mascofcte,”
now doing only reasonable business at Sydney
Criterion, convinces us that this particular opera,
is getting very, very ancient. It is so old,
that one can almost hear its aged joints crack:
in the programme, and the moss and the barnacles
have grown over it, and the footprints of.
primeval man can be seen on the pages-
of the libretta And still it would be a.
gladsome opera to this day, only it is filled with,
old, grey puns, and some of them are very bad,
and some of them are worse, and the rest are
utterly horrible. Nearly everybody considers that..
h;as a right to take ghastly liberties with the
. Mascotte,” and to put grim, fire-blasted jokes*,
into it on his own account. No one can tell,
why this is, for nobody, as a rule, puts great.
ic-Jir ?^ a^s his own ghastly merriment into.
Maritana,” or “ lolanthe,” and no common clodl
or Bloggs tries to put local gags into*
‘ Macbeth.” We want to see the “ Maseotte” as*
the author intended it to be—to see it once only;,
and then die before the unhappy opera is edited!
any more. The company at the Criterion is a
°P e > f several of the members—notably
Miss Lily Stephens, the winsome Fiametta, and
Miss Lily Everett (there are four Lilies in tlie com-
pany, all told), who plays Bettina—seem to
improve nightly. Also, the music is like the
songs of the heavenly choirs, and the scenery is
beautiful, particularly the far-off glimpse of the
azure mountains and the lakes, seen through the
back-door of the wobblesorne potentate’s palace ;
but despite these things there is still an aching
void and a yearning left unfilled. For we want to ■
find on the stairs the bleached skeleton of the man ,
who put the last batch of puns into the “Mas-
cotte,”_and to step heavily on his spine as we go •
up, and then we would be happy—happy beyond i
expression.

The best feature about George
pantomime at Sydney Majesty’s is that it is.
exceedingly like every other pantomime we ever
saw—save one which took fire and fell on top of
the principal funny man. There are whole stacks.
of glittering scenery in it, and they have as much ,
to do with the story as a black Hebrew has with.
Christmas. There are the usual local gags which ,
come in suddenly as if dragged by the hair, after the
mannerof the patent medicine man’s “advt., ’’which:
tells how the villain of the piece was carried down*
Niagara in a boat, and just as he was going over,
a cannon-ball struck him in the wind and blew,

into the rightful heir’s back-garden, and then adds-
suddenly that this reminds us of how Smith’s:
porous-plaster is the best thing for groans andl
wooden legs. There are grand spectacular effects
in which 1240 square yards of rage and
clysm and shrieksome frenzy are followed by 1280*
square yards of profound peace and summer love-

artist and the property man, and the result is a
great and shining success, which makes us wish
in our private capacity that we were dead.

On board the “ Success,” tlie wax convicts
stand silent and dead in their wooden tombs, and
look with dull eyes through the iron bars at the
visitors who come and go. Very grim and reti-
cent are these dank, dead men in rags, who roost
among the shadows like ghosts in a bath, while
their sons and grandsons and their great-grand-
daughters come and look at them through the
keyhole, and refuse to acknowledge the relation-
ship. It is a pleasant thing to stand by and watch
the live aristocrat as he sniffs contemptuously at
his own stuffed ancestor, only it is very rough on
the ancestor. But the ancient plaster argonaut
takes the insult in silence. Probably ho resolves
to lie low till ho meets his descendant in another
world, and then ho will pursue him with a pitch-
fork through the halls of gloom, arid bang his
affrighted ghost in the regions of eternal fire.

CogiU’s niggers, at Sydney Garrick, still
flourish like a green bay tig-tree, and for people
who like this variety of nigger this is exactly the
variety of nigger that they would like. The
house isn’t full, but then nothing is really full in
these times. John F. Sheridan’s Vienna Summer
Garden at Darlinghurst is as bright as before but
it is too soon to decide whether it is or is not to bo
a success. There is always a chance that the fine
old colonial gentrymen of Sydney won’t go to it
unless Jersey distinctly intimates that they may
do so without damage to their poor little stunted
dignity, and when the humble rowdy hears of
anything called a garden he always wants to howl
and blaspheme in it and get up fights. Therefore,
one-half of Sheridan’s patrons may not come, and
he may have to eject part of the other half.

The “ wonderful Japanese troupe,” the
“entranced lady,” and a mystic heathen described
as the Fakir of Oolu are the newest attractions at
Bondi Aquarium. They all help to make up a
good show, and it would be unkind to ask where
Oolu may be, and what a Fakir really is, and
what is the difference between an entranced lady
and an insensible female or an ordinary girl
asleep ? The other Aquarium at Coogee is also

at at at at
“The Merry Monarch” thrives at Mel-
bourne Princess’, although sassiety is palpably
disappointed in his royal Nibs, Aon see, he is a
small, baldheaded potentate with a sham nose,
and little of that divinity which doth hedge an
ordinary king. His merry monarchy is absolute
in some respects, and the penalty for “ plugging ”
him on his hereditary boko is sudden death, yet
his subjects don’t take their sovereign seriously,
and his foreign policy is hopelessly mixed. An
ambassador from a neighbouring court comes along
with a sweet princess, all gauze and dimples, who
seems well calculated to soothe his Majesty’s
declining years, byt the noble idiot ruins His
prospects of a happy alliance by engaging the
ambassador at single-stick whilst the damsel is
eloping with a pedlar. The king, by the way,
was born under the same star as the pedlar, whose
rumoured assassination at the outset of his elope-
ment is bound to result, according to the local
astrologer, in a royal funeral twenty-four hours
later. Consequently, the king devotes a good deal of
the third act to grieving over his approaching dis-
solution, in company with the astrologer. A special
Act of Parliament provides for the execution of
that miserable star-gazer directly his royal master
has ceased to be a destiny, which probably
accounts for the functionary’s sky-blue complexion
and melancholy smile. Towards the finish,
matters are righted by the restoration of the
pedlar to the soft, round arms of his Princess,
whereupon the King settles down for a further
term of bachelor insanity, and the Court cele-
brates the occasion with a chorus. Regarded as
an opera, “ The Merry Monarch ” is of no con-
sequence. Most of the singing falls to the throat
of Elton, in the name part, and everybody knows
that the range of William’s warble is limited.
The libretto, too, was unfortunately left to the
mercy of the man who wrote “ Evangeline,” and
a plot that offers great opportunities for humour
is spoilt by the author’s inability to put in
worthy dialogue or quaint situations. As
a pantomime, however, the show is fitfully funny,
and Elton does his best to make it go like that
other one-man entertainment, “The Old Guard.”
All the same, he doesn’t quite succeed. The inci-
dental passage at arms with single-sticks is wildly
funny, especially on nights when both combatants
get well set, but almost as much hilarity may be
got out of a clown and policeman when their
eyes are “in,” and the leg of mutton is hurled
with unerring aim. George Lauri, who makes his
first appearance as the irate ambassador, plays a
generally heavy character in a way that promises
well; Howard Yernon, as the astrologer, is a
dismal suggestion of the Lord High Executioner,
in “Mikado,” and Charley Ryley impersonates
the pedlar in a pleasant but perfunctory
nianner. Florence Young is every way charm-
ing when she gets the chance, Violet Yarley
also contributes to the total in a smaller
degree, whilst the general display of limbs and
costumes is noticeable, even just now when the
market is flooded with such goods. Scenery is not
above the Princess’s high average, and a Nautch
dance appeals to the eye of artists who study
curves and outlines rather than to the senses of
the gallery,

At the Alexandra they have been doing
satisfactory business with “Jack the Giant
Killer,” and the efforts of a rather serious-minded
company are requited with whistles that startle

the transformation-scene has acquired a certain
amount of romantic interest from the fact that a
demented girl recently poisoned herself all on
account of “Alta” Tischbauer, who painted it.
When limelight falls upon the splendour it seems
like the young person’s radiant smile from realms
above.

The costly, florid magnificence of “ Much
Ado” is duly appreciated at Melbourne Bijou,
where the waits have been reduced to reasonable
dimensions, and the curtain falls upon a lot of.
imminent nuptials on the right side of 11 p.m.
Familiarity with this elegant spectacle breeds
contempt tor nobody but the Divine Bard, a gen-
tleman who was evidently not well up in the
religious etiquette of his period. Otherwise, he
wouldn’t have allowed Claudio and the Prince to
make a disgusting family scandal in a cathedral,
or have imagined the possibility of the chief priest
clearing out with the bride and her father so that
Beatrice might take up a commanding position in
front of the altar and bid Benedick bring her a bit
of the false bridegroom’s gizzard as a love-token.
The scene was an insult to Holy Church, and
absurd, anyway. A couple ■ of vergers or
monks would have run the profaners out of
the tabernacle, and very likely the Heavens would
have tumbled in before the Pope had time to curse
the whole wedding-party off the face of the earth.
This is the Bard’s affair, however. Brough and
Boucicault are not to blame for this absurd row in
the sanctuary. They are, however, in a measure
accountable for Cecil Ward’s hat, which is pain-
fully remindful of a draper’s clearing sale, and for
those same ill-fitting silk knickers that Tithehidge
wore in “Devotion.” No matter. Allowing a
margin for stage license “ Much Ado ” is eminently
satisfactory, although there is nothing in the per-
formance to touch one’s modern heart.
