George Coppin and his brother directors
of Melbourne Royal have dealt severely with the
free-list, which of late assumed more and more
tlie appearance of a tax levied upon shows by the
local press. A scrutiny of “ names on the door ”
revealed the appalling fact that 1(5 men from the
Argus and 14 from the Age were down as
“ critics ” for those organs, on the strength of one
first notice that is generally regarded as mislead-
ing drivel, followed by occasional paragraphs
which no intelligent reader gives heed to. The
free admissions claimed by a host of other prints,
down to the puniest of weeklies, were even more
numerous in proportion to influence,’ and the
result of this Xmas investigation was an all-round
notice to the-effect that-none but the working
critic from each paper would be welcomed at the
Royal for the next month. Moreover, to avoid
any misunderstanding, the management sent one
ticket for Boxing.Nig.ht to the several editors. In
thus closing the list against lady companions of
men on the job Ooppin & Co. made a questionable
departure from precedent, inasmuch as some
critics require the moral and physical support of
a wife, especially at the pantomime. The intro-
duction of this “one man, one notice” policy
was, however, so evidently the outcome of
a disgusting abuse of the list system that
a sense of common decency should have
persuaded the offending papers to swallow their
corrective pill without a murmur. Evidently
there isn’t much common decency about the
almighty Age, which rose in its wrath and imme-
diately instructed its whole gang of “representa
tives,” including the one man honestly entitled to
free admission, to pay their way into the theatre,
an order which, by-the-by, in nowise altered the
position of thq other thirteen, who were already
barred at the door. So far, so fair. The Age
critic bought his ticket for the Royal on Boxing
Night, and produced a notice strictly in accord-
ance, doubtless, with the merits of the show as
judged by an angry, person of moderate intelli-'
gence. Brethren of the praft who conjectured
that the Age would “slate” the entertainment
were, The Bulletin imagines, founding such an
assumption upon their own inner consciousness of
journalistic etiquette, and not upon .official
evidence. But any Melbournian who oftres to
place the Age notice of the pantomime at the
Alexandra (where the “list” flourishes luxuriantly
as ever) side by side with the Age notice of the
pantomime at the Royal, will be able to form a
good estimate of the value of moat press
at * * *
Coppin has carried his war with the Age
into the enemy’s camp by cutting down the Royal
advt. to two foolish proceeding, anyway.
The Age is without question the best advertising
medium in Melbourne, and if a six-inch, or bigger,
space in other dailies is a good business invest-
ment for the theatre, evidently Coppin must lose
more than David Syme by the reduction of (fie
order in this particular instance. Also, in making
such an invidious distinction between one paper
that butters freely, and another that merely puts
a “scrape” into its notice, the Royal director
gives the public a choice between two surmises—
either that lie is cutting off his nose to spite his
face, or obe that ho regards the cost of all advo v
tiseinents exceeding two inches in each paper as
cash payment for eulogistic notices.

The season of Chinese drama in Melbourne
is bringing out plenty of new talent. A three-act
play of most modern topical interest filled the
Chow bill last Saturday, for instance, and must
have been slung together by some resident genius
within the last month or so. The trouble opened
with the arrival of an English missionary,
his wife, and a brace of redeemed lepers, at a
Chinese town, represented by a few yards of
tapestry hanging from a clothes-line. Presently
a couple of Celestial “slops” slunk to the fore
and arrested the pious Britisher for insulting
behaviour, whereupon one of the converted
heathen slew a “slop” for the advancement of
Christianity. However, the local authorities
grabbed the missionary and put him on trial,
and that was where the real strength of Chinese
drama came in. Virtue wasn’t triumphant.
Nobody turned up to prove - that the mis-
sionary was a decent man in disguise, so that
the Court calmly and monotonously sentenced
him to death. The Emperor granted no free
pardon ; there was no attempt to rescue the mis-
sionary ; his two pet converts silently deserted
him in the hour of trouble, and lie merely died
like a dawg in a barrel full of spikes, with his face
turned to the blazing sun and his eyelids cut off
short. That brought the drama to a plain, prac-
tical conclusion. A missionary had been caught
in the ordinary way, and killed in the usual
manner, and the orchestra celebrated the occasion
bv playing the darndest music ever heard out of
Sheol.

The late Alfred Cellier, as a comic opera composer,
stood in the position of understudy to Sir Arthur Sulli-
van. His music is quainter and more scholarly than such
as Edward Solomon’s, I ut it lacks the deptlFand clever
appropriateness to the text of his patron’s work. Cellier
was very much in touch with Sullivan’s style, however,
and is understood to have filled in bits of musical back-
ground for “lolanthe,” and other of the Gilberlian
series. Cellier, who died at George Mnsgrove’s house in
London, probably succumbed through the bursting of a
blood-vessel.' The poor fellow, one of the most charming
of men, was far advanced in consumption when last he
visited Australia.

An originator of imitations, as you may say. He will,

Sydney’s partially-built New Lyceum Theatre figured
in the Central Police Ciurt last week in a case of criminal
libel brought by the agent of the ground-landlords
against Mr. J. C. Low- publisher of the Builders and
Contractors News. The article complained of called the
landlords “scoundrels” and “social skunks.” When the
case was called on, Mr. Low made a public apology and
agreed to pay £B4 costs, and, ponding the last condition
being carried out, he was remanded for three weeks.

on 18th Nov. Ip a crowded house. The company includes
Sophie Harris, Nina Tulloch (in her Australian magpie
act, which is a great success), Misses Dewhurst and Raze
(most attractive dancers), Connie Salisbury, Bessie Free-
man (a sweet mezzd-poprano), Vincinni (a clever violin
soloist), and Kate Rickards ; Messrs. Pierce and Sheridan,
and Harry himself, who'has a host of new sketches and
songs. After doing a six w eeks’ season here, they go to
Pretoria, then on to Natal, Port Elizabeth and Capetown,
leaving for x\ustralia in March. In Capetown, seven new
artists will join Harry for his Australian campaign.
Bernard Shaw, in his “Quintessence of Ibsenism,”
cuttingly remarks The ‘dramatic critic’ of a news-
paper, in ordinary circumstances, is at his best a good
descriptive reporter, and at' His worst a mere theatrical
newsman. As such, he is a person of importance among
actors and managers, and of no importance whatever
elsewhere. Naturally, he frequents the circles in which
alone he is made much of; and by the time he has seen so
many performances that he has formed a critical standard
in spite of himself, he has also enrolled among his per-
sonal acquaintances every actor and manager of a few
years’ standing, and become engaged in all the private
likes and dislikes, the quarrels and friendships - in a -word,
in all the partialities which personal relations involve, at
which point the value of his verdicts may be imagined.
Add to this that if he has the misfortune to be attached
to a paper in which theatrical advertisements are an
object, or of which the editor and proprietor's (or their
wives) do not hesitate to incur obligations to managers
by asking for complimentary admissions, he may often
have to choose between making himself agreeable and
forfeiting his post. So that he is not always to he relied
on even as a newsman where the plain truth would give
offence to any individual.”
In company once they were debating who had
played the most characters in “Hamlet,” and some were
inclined to plump for Harwood, but Cathcart rapped in
with his Hamlet, Ghost, King, Polonius, Laertes, Horatio,
Osric, Player King, Rosencrantz, and was going on to the
crack of doom, when they cried—" Hold, enough !”
Fred. Thomas, who used to canoodle through his epi-
glottis, ventriloquially “had the loan ” of many up-country
railway employes, by the aid of his far - away engine
shriek. He would invite the station-master over to
imbibe, and as the liquor was elevated preparatory to
“surroundation,” a screech from au engine would be
heard a quarter of a mile away. The sherbet was
dropped—not inside the diaphragm—and the station
made in even time, amid general laughter. The ruse
worked always.

What is Stanley doing?
“Jim” Byers had a “season” of two nights recently at
Bithurst, when he produced his own sensation, “The
Dagworth Mystery,” assisted by local amateurs. The fun
was fast and furious, Jim being a target for all the stale
jokes of the district, while the private lives of the
amateurs engaged became public properly.
There is grave reason to fear that the damnable heresies
of 19th century latitudinarianism have penetrated
into the S.M. Herald establishment. The attention of
the Chief Inquisitor is respectfully directed to a recent
Echo notice of “ Faust up to Date,” in which the writer
refers to “the immense strides made by civilisation since
the days when the existence of a personal devil was an
article of faith with our forefathers.” This awful insinua-
tion that Au!d Clootie is an allegory or figment of sacer-
dotalism ought to entail a visit of the Pitt-street familiars,
and at least a chastening from the secular hand of “ Mr.
James.” To doubt the substantiality of the devil is
almost as bad as to question the divine origin of Free-
trade, or tire reality of Tophet. Wherefore the Holy
Office should look to’t.
Here is the story of how Parnell’s liaison with
Mrs. O’Shea first became known to certain mem-
bers of the Irish party. After the Phcenix Park
murders, his colleagues vainly searched for him: —
What frenzied consultation —what agonised conjectures
what despairful proposals this time brought forth ! At
last the report came that Mr. Parnell had gohe to Paris,
and some of his colleagues went over there to see hiih.
Mr. Patrick Egan, the treasurer of the Land League, was
a resident in that city at the time, and thus a council of
the Land League could be held there ; but in the hotel
where Parnell was expected to be there was neither trace
nor announcement of him. But several letters awaited
him. There were all kinds of horrid suspicions. It was
known that some of his relatives had ended tragically,
and there was always, in those moments of crises,-the
dreadful feeling that anything might happen to Parnell.
After a solemn consultation it was decided to open some
of the letters, in the hope of finding some trace or clue to
the vanished chief. One of the letters was fiom a lady;
it was scarcely glanced at; but it told enough ; it was the
first warning the Irish party had of the opening of the
tragedy that finally engulfed Parnell and went near to
engulfing Ireland. It speaks for the reticence
and the delicacy which—in spite of all their supposed
garrulousness and the brutality sometimes of their
political controversy—really distinguished Irishmen—
that this fact was never communicated for years after-
wards by the men who knew it, even to their intimate and
close friends in the same party ; and that the vast majority
of the members of the party never heard of it at ail.
It is T. P. O’Connor who tells this. Now, if he
and friends opened Parnell’s letters, the latter
(unless the letters were destroyed and nothing
said about them) must have become aware that
his colleagues shared his secret. However, Mr.
O’Connor says nothing on this point, although it
is a point on which the public have an imperative
right to be informed ; they have also a right to be
informed how it came about that a set of men who
were on merely political terms with their leader
dared to open his private letters at all—especially
private letters addressed in a woman’s hand-
writing. The calm impudence of these people in
now taking credit to themselves for the reticence
they displayed concerning the observations made
by i hem during the commission of a social burglary
is something to marvel at. But whatever the omis-
sions from the littlebook, it is not only admirable as
a journalistic tour dc force, but as a just apprecia-
tion of Parnell’s greatness. The sale of the volume
has been small in Australia, because the Irish, for
one cause, think “ Tay Pay ” could, at this junc-
ture, say nothing but what is bad of the dead
chieftain. The idea is erroneous ; for no book yet
written gives so lucid a revelation of Parnell’s
great heart and intense, though narrow, intellect.

folly to talk of honesty being the best policy in

Gov. Kintore told the boys at an Adelaide
prize-giving, that “ the colonies” would be guilty
of suicidal folly in attempting to set up for them-
selves. All right. If we suicide, it’s only our
own funeral.
John Stewart, the vet., is the most regular
attendant and the most silent man in the N.S.W.
Legislative Council. The Hon. Lord hasn’t been
seen in the Chamber for years, though he lives
within a mile of it.
“The labouring classes have able repre-
sentatives on the council of the N.S.W. (bogus)

Take up Carlyle’s French Revolution,
apropos of France in 178!), and you have Russia
in 1891, even to the parallel passage where hungry
complainants were hanged on a gallows 40' feet
high, and the people ate grass.
Parkes is offering a sham opposition to the
adoption of the Dibbs tariff. His motive is not
quite apparent; but probably he welcomes the
advent of “the creature,” as it will give him a
distinct cry to stump the province on.
The St. Leonards (N.S.W.) Labour Elec-
toral League have asked the central committee for
an official definition of the phrase “sinking the
fiscal question.” They might as well ask for a
lock of hair from the tail of the last comet.
John Bray, the new S.A. Agent-General,
is an Australian native —who glories in a tinpot
title. Knutsford is sure to get on nicely with a
knighted native who stands condemned as a snob
from personal choice, rather than as the victim of
early Hinglish associations.
There is only one way to bring about a
union of England and Australia after all. Let
England be dug up and sent out here in bags, and
made into a continuation of Cape Schank, and
then the two lands will become one, and the
silken bonds will be fixed up at last.
In certain quarters the question of the
hour is, is Griffith adroitly playing with the Com-
monwealth Bill until Lilley throws up the Chief
Justiceship, or is he doubtful of his ability to carry
the province on his imperial hobby, and still
keep the reversion of the Chief Justiceship in his
hands ?
Munro lost no time in contradicting the

appointed Agent-General directly he set foot in
Victoria. Perhaps Munro may further affront
the influential organ by refusing to fulfil its
prediction that he will take over Berry’s billet
himself.

Parkes is, on the whole, acting fairly
up to his declaration of 1887: —“If the Pro-
tectionists be in the majority, I must of necessity

In all the Australias, save Victoria and
Queensland, the Chief Justice is Lieutenant-
Governor. In those two provinces, the occupants
of the chief seats on the Bench are too Radical to
be entrusted by the Imperial authorities with
what is nominally the highest position now attain-
able by a local citizen.
Lord Brassey, with whom Parkes, Gillies,
and Griffith are alleged to be acting in concert,
proposes that Australia’s naval tribute to
Britannia shall be quadrupled in the interest of
“the commerce of the antipodes.” And “the
-adequate naval defence ” is to be supplied by
England’s obsolete warships !
The politico-social dynamiters who write
to (or rather in) the Sydney daily papers raising
the sectarian cry are, The Bulletin notices, always
anonymous. By the way, the latest bloody-shirt
demonstration appears in Sydney D.T., and
is signed “Argus.” It would have been ever so
much more honest to sign it “Daily Telegraph.”
Mr. Ogilvie, in the Westminster Review
for September, notes that in Australia, where
wages have hitherto been high, the rate of increase
of population is only about 2 per cent, per annum.
The lower the stage of civilisation, short of bar-
barism, the more fecund the people. As brain-
power is cultivated the reproductive power
diminishes.

pi B retired from politics, Reid is now nominal
leader of the Opposition, Sir Patrick Jennings is
in the u pper House, and Abbott is Speaker of the
Legislative Assembly.

nicking this year on the banks of the Derwent,

Missionaries from the New Hebridean
group complain bitterly that the “ French
authorities ’’ are chowsing the pore heathens out of
their land, instead of leaving it to be religiously
acquired by the church. It would seem that the
missionaries have turned up the good work in
consequence. What shall it profit a man to
save the soul of a savage who has lost his free-
hold rights?
The first movement in the direction of
providing the associated capitalists of Australia
with “Pinkerton Guards ” was the enrolment of
special constables (many of them afterwards paid
by Government) during the late strike. The
second step in a similar direction is the engage-
ment by the Pastoralists’ Association of a horde
of private detectives for secret service in Queens-
land and elsewhere.
is it consistent with the spirit of the Con-

of the Parliament should be superior to the Par-
liament i i surely an anomaly in a democratic
community. No man can serve two masters, yet
here is the Tory Darley, C.J., employed by
aristocratic England and democratic N.S.W.

Ex-Governor Caringfcon, a well-informed
London correspondent writes us, has rather over-
shot the mark in England. Even his Radical
friends are blaming him for his blundering tactics
as well as his astounding ignorance of political
history. Granville’s bad policy in dealing with
the colonies is far too recent to be forgotten, and
to say that all the Colonial Office blundering is
the work of the Tories is too strong even for
Radical stomachs.
Sir Julian Salomons is a-recruiting in the
East. It is said that he has paired for all
divisions on the One-man-one-vote Bill. His'
deportment on the Xmas adjournment was racial.
His chief wished the members of the Assembly a
Merry Xmas, but hesaid not a word on the subject to
the political derelicts in the Council. His absence
from the leadership of the Council will be a big
advantage to the Dibbs Cabinet. For Salomons
is a crotchetty character, who can seldom speak
the word that turneth away wrath. On the other
hand, Dick O’Connor is a peacemaker; and his
tact and tongue may avert the threatened strangu-
lation of the Electoral Bill.
The “keystone of the democratic arch”
and Premier Dibbs have drawn their political
swords on one another in the face of the enemy.
The unwitting cause of the estrangement of the
two Protectionists is Major Parrott, an officer of
the Soudan Contingent, whose merits once com-
manded the appreciative attention of Dibbs when
he was sitting in Opposition. Dibbs, it would
seem, promised O’Sullivan that he would find a
billet of some sort for Parrott, who is suffering
from the res augusta dorni. Unfortunately for his
friend, O’Sullivan, in his new-born zeal for econo-
mising the expenditure, ridiculed Dibbs’ military
estimates in the press. This raised Dibbs’ dander,
and lit* lost all interest in Parrott; and now Dibbs
and O'Sullivan are not playing political chaynies.
Dear Bulletin, —The present N.S. VV.
Ministry must not be content to rest under
the cowardly imputations of its anonymous
clerical enemies in the press —to the effect
that because it happens to contain _ two
Roman Catholics (out of nine members) it is
engaged in a corrupt conspiracy to destroy the
secular education system. The, Ministry must be
aggressive : it must carry the war into the camp
of its enemies. It must nail secular education to
the masthead —it must work in the direction of
completely secularising education ; it must make
education absolutely free in order that it may be
justly compulsory. An excellent opportunity of
making a trumpet-like pronouncement upon the
subject will arise in connection with Niuiau Mel-
ville’s free-education resolution. —A.N. A.
The freetrade bogie of “the priest in
politics in N.S.W.” reminds The Bulletin of the
fact that “ the parson ” has played a considerable
part in that line without raising a hue-and-cry
against any particular sect. In the S.M. Herald
is entombed the work of the-Revs. John West,
Garvan, W. Curnow, H. Woolnough, W. Ward,
J. Osborne, and J. E. Cullen, &c., &c., &e. The
Empire published the political platform of the
Revs. J. D. Laing, B. Quaife, and David Blair;
the Daily Telegraph retained the services of the
Revs. F. W. Ward, J. Osborne, J. R. Crone, and
Charles Bright, and the more than reverend
“Bob” Nall; and the same with the Evening
News. Why, in the Herald office, there’s a
“ tradition ” that no irreverent pressman has ever
been paid for a leading article.
The Yictorian religious census is just
out, gladdening those denominations which poll.
Of course Mat Larkin is in one, G. N. Taylor in
another, Tommy the Nut in another, for the
supply of infidels is simply ridiculous—only 500
and odd Atheists in the whole country !
You have to look microscopically for un-
believers of any kind in the census, just as in
any church, and the wonder is that Bishop Moor-
house was able to complain, at the Rhyl Church
Congress this year, of the abolition of State aid to
religion in Australia. Now, does not this census
afford convincing reasons why it should be the
last ? Monstrous humbug —no heathens in
the community, the millennium begun! Surely the
denominations themselves ought to scout it, for
even the very gaols are carefully ticketed off into
the 400 sects, with their insects.
If Dibbs requires any warrant for advising
Governor Jersey to increase the strength of the
N.S.W. Legislative Council, it is obtainable in a
despatch by Earl Granville to Governor Bel-
more ;
On certain critical occasions it may become not only ex-
pedient but indispensable to bring the two houses into
harmony by creating or threatening to create, a number
of legislative councillors sufficient for that purpose !
\\ ill the denial of the will of the people, as re-
gards no plural voting, constitute “a critical occa-
sion ?” Anyhow, Dibbs has the authority of
Parkes, as expressed in the Assembly ten years
ago, for appointing 11 additional members. Parkes
has ever contended that the number of Councillors
should keep pace with the number of the people’s
representatives, and that the Council should be con-
stituted so as to give the Government of the day
“afair working majority.”
The cable reports an explosion of gun-
cotton at Dublin Castle “ in a room underneath
the chamber in which the Privy Council intended
to meet shortly.” What is “shortly”? If a
chimney has tottered off its basis and will shortly
fall on thfe head of the man underneath it means
about three seconds, but if the end of the world is
coming shortly it means about 40 billion years.
There is a solemn thrill about the idea of a gun-
cotton explosion underneath a Privy Council that
was going to meet shortly, but after all the
Council might have only intended meeting in 18
months, and an explosion under these conditions
means just as much as a fire in the house that is
going to be built next century. It will probably
be found, when the cablernan gets over the Kris-
mish holidays that the explosion was due to
sewer-gas. The old pile is built on the edge of
the river Poddle, now one of the main sewers, as
the old Tank-stream is to Sydney.
Till a few years ago the N.S. W. Legisla-
tive Assembly regularly declined to consider a
1 rotectionist resolution. A question of fiscal
reform, whenever raised by Buchanan or Melville
—the only Protectionists in the 10th Parliament—
was at once the signal to the Parkesian whip to
organise a count-out. For instance, in 1881
Buchanan invited the House to relieve the
‘farmers, mechanics, and manufacturers from
the pressure of foreign competition,” The House,
after being re-formed four times in 15 minutes,
counted out at 8 o’clock. Of the 111 members of
that anti-Protectionist House only 24 sit in the
present Assembly, 15 on the Protectionist side
and nine on the Freetrade side. And of the 15
Protectionists seven are ministers, and two are
o.beers of the House. Dibbs didn’t sit in the
Parliament of 1881.
J rom the Illustrated Penny Paper :
It is an interesting fact that Lord Rosebery may, if the
Progressives come to power in the County Council at the
next election, bo succeeded by Lord Carington, who has
now developed into a statesman of real ability and mark.
He has lately come out at Bristol as a pronounced
Radical, and he will probably stand for the Hoxton
division of Shoreditch. He is a fresh and brilliant
speaker, full of shrewdness, with good looks, good
manners, and plenty of wealth. Moreover, his ideas have
xr e | n w hol '°. Ugh,y democratised during his Governorship of
JN.b.vy. where he worked in his shirt-sleeves during the
lecent flood to save the people’s homes from destruction,
and made an excellent personal impression all round.
He is a coming man in politics, and I would advise my
readers to keep their e3’es on him.
So, Carington is “a fresh and brilliant speaker ”
—and the British public should keep their eyes on
him ! The Bulletin can understand the English
admiring and trusting the easy and spontaneous
Rosebery, wit, scholar, and sportsman, a man
among men. But that a big nation should be
solemnly told to “keep its eye” (in such a sense)
on the heartless, tricky, shallow, downy, trans-
parent Carington—fangh ! The illustrated paper
again.

A returned Maorilander thus delivers
himself in an Auckland (M.L.) paper:—
During iiiy stay in Sydney I tried for two and thirty
situations that were advertised in the local papers. lii
one instance there were no less than 1700 applicants for
one situation ; in another there were 430 applicants. T
could keep on enumerating similar cases where there
were hundreds after the same situation. I met lots of
Maonlanders unable to obtain any work, and who
would give their labour free over there to obtain food
alone. Hundreds sleep in the Domain at night. I saw
advertisements where young men were offering their
li r? ur for foo( * a l° ,le - One advertisement ran thus •
Distressed, will give his labour free for food alone.”
Although I have travelled in different parts of the world
I have never seen so much poverty as I saw durin°* mv
stay in Sydney. Sydney is a fine place, there is not the
least doubt about that, but men cannot live without
employment; and should any of your numerous readers
want to better themselves, do not go to Sydney, I say.
Robert Reid, Melbourne warehouseman,
has left for England to represent Melbourne
Chamber of Commerce at London Conference of
the world’s Chambers of Commerce, and this is
what he says he is going to say
The feeling of every true Australian is as truly British,
and with the old country we shall sink or swim. lam no
separationist, nor do I believe in the idle talk of people
who suggest that one day the two countries will part
company.
In other words, Reid enthusiastically refuses to
accept the lessons of history. He is an imported
article who visits England too often and has
become wealthy too rapidly to become politi-
cally acclimatised. He no more represents
Australian public opinion than General Gourko
represents the public opinion of the Russian
steppes.
A social difficulty of the same old des-
cription is reported from Brisbane. A legal
gentleman got married a short time ago, and
built a bower for himself and the object of his
affections not far from the shining river, so that
they could embrace each other in the garden on
dark evenings, and listen to the gentle murmur
of the stream. He had been in a state of profound
joy for a few weeks, and then one day he was
telephoned for _at his office, and urged to come
home on the instant to avert murder and un-
speakable horror, because a strange female wanted
to scatter his wife on the doorstep. He went
home at a run, and found that anarchy had
broken loose. There were a woman and four
children in hysterics in the garden, and the
woman was trying to shove the offspring through
the keyhole, and claimed that the house and the
husband were both hers, and that she was a
deceived widow whose life had been laid waste.
The door was locked, and a pallid bride was
looking with a glassy expression through the top
window. The bride’s mother, who had evidently
been sent for by way of a reinforcement, was
expostulating through another window, and the
slavey was giving “back talk” to the enraged
visitor from the further side of the keyhole. The
bower, in short, was a desolation, and’ just as the
owner rushed through the gate the woman in the
front garden began to tell things about his past
life which made the blood of the whole neighbour-
hood run cold. He headed her off in the midst of
her revelations by means of a £2O note, and she
retreated sobbing down the road with the four
children, and then he went in and tried to explain
things to the family. But somehow the bower
has never been the same since that visitation, and
apparently it never will, for the trail of the
visitor is over the flowers and their bloom has
departed.

Mr. Evelyn Snodgrass has taken holy
orders at Melbourne. He is a son of Peter Snod-
grass, an old-time Victorian legislator, and
grandson of Colonel Snodgrass, who was Acting-
Governor of N.S.W. between the departure of
Governor Bourke and the arrival of Governor
Gipps.
W. Hammett, in 1830 leader of the Dorset-
shire revolt against the introduction of farming
machinery, and sentenced to transportation for
life to Australia, after serving many years of his
sentence was released and returned home a
broken man, to end his days in the poorhouse.
His death, after years of pauperdom, has just been
announced.
Old Bill Crook, a well - nigh forgotten
murderer, died in Geelong Gaol last week, aetat
80. The gaol at “ the Pivot ” is a sort of
Benevolent Asylum for used-up assassins, and in
the “ lifer ” yard of that drowsy institution you
can see groups of mild-looking ancients, each one
of whom bears the brand of Cain covered by warts
and wrinkles.

Kent) to beget his father’s successor to the Throne.

unguarded moment, that he doesn’t expect the
British Monarchy to see him through to his royal
end,
Bingham Crowther, physician, “ etc.,”

time, and the Crowthers got what they required—
bold advertisement.
H. J, Wrixon, of Victoria, and John
Colton, of South Australia, have been officially-
damned this week with the title of K.C.M.G.
Also, her Majesty has been graciously pleased t<>
partially damn Captain Moore and E. G. Fitz-
gibbon with the title of C.M.G. In other words,
the British monarch has branded Wrixon and
Colton in front with a large hot flat-iron, and has
branded Fitzgibbon from behind with a small one
that wasn’t quite so hot.
G. H. Haydon, discoverer of Gippsland
from the Melbourne side, died in London on 9th
November, aged 70. He was once secretary to
the Bridewell and Bethlehem Hospitals, and
arrived in Melbourne in 1839. A sketch he made
of Melbourne in 1840 is familiar in engravings.
He went with a party to Gippsland, struck the
coast opposite French Island, returned to England
in 1845, and published in 1853 a book called
“Five Years in Australia.” Thenceforward he
devoted his mind to the antiquities of London.
It is related of the late millionaire John
Harris, of Shane’s Park, N.S.W., that he once,
because of personal hate, spent £5OOO in defeating
a namesake for West Sydney, when he made one
speech, as follows Well, boys, you all know
me, and the only speech I’m going to make to
yer is. ‘ Come in, an’ have a drink.’ ” The
Cumberland Times, published in his recent locale,
gives the old man an unlovely character in the
main and says that when urged to make a will he
invariably replied; “Divil a bit o’ a will I’ll
make. Let the s fight for it when I’m done
wi’ it.”

Wo have received the following letter,
si gin d by “Mary Roberts” and “Win. Roberts”: —
Editor Bulletin, —Owing to a malicious letter appearing
in ,\our journal of 2SUi Nov., signed by “ S.K., ’ reflecting
upon the action of the friends of the late J. O. Wascoe
in “ ignoring their late relative’s wishes hy burying him
in the C.K. cemetery,” we most emphatically repudiate
“S.E.’s” assertion, and are prepared to disprove the
statement. As a matter of fact, Mr. Wascoe died a
Protestant, which faith he had always followed. This
fact alone precluded any such wish being gratified, even
had it been expressed.
Mesto i is making another expedition to
the Bel ten den Ker Mountains, accompanied
this time by the two Italian collectors to
whom he is acting as cicerone. A prophet
is unhonoured in his own country, and Cairns
people do not read Meston’s rhetoric with the
enthusiasm of Southerners. Hp was dilating
the other day on the difficulty of his famous
assent, and calculating the obstacles to its repe-
tition. “Why don’t you take a couple of pack-
horses?” said Jack O’Leary, the clerk to Cairns
Divisional Board, “ there’s a good track clear up
to the top.”

An ingenious London expert, who has
made a study of the legal tests applied to deter-
mine the genuineness of milk—fat solids and solids
other than fat, &c.—has invented a fraud that
beats the analysis every time. By adding a-third
of “ separated ” milk to a quantity of new milk,
the mixture keeps the standard of “fat solids,’’
lyut has such an excess of “ solids other than fat ”
that it will receive about 5 per cent, of water and
then answer all the test conditions. Thus, 300
gallons of new milk, plus 100 gallons of separated
milk, plus 20 gallons of water, can be legally sold
as 420 gallons of wholesome new milk that will
satisfactorily respond to all the legal requirements.
A law has been passed in New York State
whereby a tombstone-cutter, if his bill isn’t paid
within six months after the funeral, can root up
the emblem of grief from its place above the dust
of the deceased, and dispose of it as lie thinks
best. This is a good move, for it isn’t nice to see
insolvent tombstone-cutters gritting their teeth in
the cemetery, and shaking their bony fists at the
inscriptions above the unpaid-for dead. And
another good idea would be toauthoriae any dentist,
who can’t get his money, to call round at the end
of a reasonable period, and take back the gold
stopping that he put into his customer’s molar.
Think of the rage of the dentist who gets into a
jovial company of (say) seven, and knows that six
of them are smiling with his teeth ! How he must
long to get up there and then, and seize the whole
six sets, and depart with them, leaving his un-
profitable clients to grin on a cash basis with their
own shrivelled gums !
Starting with a capital of £17,000,000, the
associated banks of Australia have, in one genera-
tion, divided £50,000,000 among their shareholders
and accumulated a reserve-fund of £31,000,000.
The Tasmanian silver-mines, at Zeehau
and Dundas, have up to now almost ruined every
Tasmanian who has touched them. A month ago
it was unanimously agreed, at meetings of the
various companies, to suspend operations sine die,
and it was stated that the total amount, in calls,
amounted to £25,010 or £30,000 per month. r i his
was too much for the pockets of the Tasmanians.
The Silver Queen Mine is the only one that has
paid a dividend, add this a very small one.
Remini is the nearest port of shipment, situated
12 miles from Mount Zeehan, and £7 10s. is paid
for carting ore this distance, over a road 12 feet
wide, which for nine months in the year is a
floating mass of corduroy. The Silver King
Mine, upon which is erected the most expensive
and complete machinery on the field, is still sink-
ing in search of the lode. The shares of this mine
which a short time ago were quoted at £llO arc
now selling at £4(>. One of the “ biggest jobs ”
ever perpetrated in the Australian colonies is the
construction of the Zeehan-Strahan railway-line.
A Black-tracker—Crick.
Sydney D.T. remarks solemnly (at the
instigation, no doubt, of some land-grab mis-
sionary or other) that the French are acquiring
“large tracts of land” in the New Hebrides by
“questionable means.” If the D.T. can point
out any large tract of land that was acquired any-
where by any other means, this paper will eat its
printing machine.
A Tasmanian Judge lately sentenced a
Chinaman to 10 years for causing the death of an
old woman of 70, by beating her. The old lady
was stealing John’s cabbages, in the middle of the
night, and the Celestial evidently never meant to
kill her. When sentenced, John said, “Ten years
too muchee, inuchee.” The noble and upright
Judge’s admonitions to the poor devil (who, by
the way, did not understand English !) were a
caution to snakes.
A Melbourne detective, accused of being
drunk when he appeared before a board of inquiry
the other day, explained that his state of fuddle
was the result of a long search for “ information,”
during which he “ had to do a great deal of treat-
ing.” So many people ’ad a glass along o’ ’im
that the liquor ultimately got into his gigantic
brain, and there wasn’t sufficient time for him to
sleep it off before facing the aforesaid board.
Under these distressing circumstances the tec’s
trifling indiscretion has no doubt been pardoned,
but the question naturally arises—what is the
precise value of “information” collected by an
active and intelligent officer when he is drunk?
Ah Kim, the Cooktown Chinese trader
who was murdered some time ago on Normanby
Island, appears to have met his death through his
irresistible personal attractions. It seems that
the wife of a local native, named Gumamumunuia,
saw Ah Kim, and at once was smitten by him.
In an evil moment she confessed to her husband
the fatal passion that Ah Kim’s charms of person
had roused in her bosom. Whereupon Mr.
Gumamumunuia, accompanied by five or six
friends, boarded Ah Kim’s vessel to remonstrate
with him. Poor Ah Kim, quite unconscious of
the havoc he had caused by his good looks, was
quietly coming out of the hold of his little craft,
when Mr. G. thrust a spear into his back. Ah
Kim managed to get hold of his gun, and G. and
his friends went over the side, but the gallant
Chow contrived to pot one in the water. His
crew sailed the vessel to Duchess Island, where
he died of his wound. Good-looking men stand
no chance in New Guinea. How, we wonder, did
the late Baron Miklouho Maclay escape ?
At a London inquest the other day it came
out that Edward Haynes, 50, cooper, out of work
and friendless, who died of pneumonia (conse-
quent on exposure) in the workhouse, had walked
from Tunbridge Wells in search of work and had
slept for six nights at the Congregational Union’s
free shelter. An attendant at the shelter deposed
that there were 200 men there every night. “ They
had no covering except what they took with them.
There were chairs for them to sit on, or they
would lie on the floor. Being asked by the coroner
if there were plenty of customers even for this
accommodation, the witness created a sensation
by replying, ‘ Yes, sir; we turned away 00 last
night.’ The shelter, supported by private sub-
scription, is merely to prevent men lying about
the streets, and the occupants, it appears, have
no food given them except on Sundays, when the
number reaches 400. The majority are dock men.
One hundred thousand persons were in the shelter
from January to October.” In the same paper it
is recorded that in a burglary case, the prisoner
said that he came out of gaol on the oth of the
month and on the loth he committed the crime.
He could get no work, and had taken the boots
off his feet and sold them for eightpence to get
tea. Do you not marvel at the patience of the
dogged Englishman ?
To counteract the enervating, relaxing,
and evil effects of the heat and depressing nature
of the climate in this country during the summer
months, no remedy has ever appeared before the
public to equal, or even that would bear com-
parison with, Clements Tonic. It prevents fever,
malaria and ague, keeps up the appetite, assists
digestion and assimilation, stimulates the stomach,
liver, kidneys, and bowels; thus preventing liver
and kidney disease, and warding of their un-
pleasant symptoms, as headache, constipation,
piles, gout, rheumatism, dropsy, apoplexy, &c.
The summer is the time when Clements Tonic is
the medicine par excellence for the people. It
prevents the relaxation and depression so prevalent
in the hot weather in these latitudes; it enriches
the blood, aids perspiration, and restores all lost
nerve-power; is the only reliable anti-febrifuge
and pick-me-up prepared in the colonies, and can
be depended upon to do good and cure disease.
The action of Clements Tonic is certain, sure, and
prompt; relief is felt immediately after taking the
first dose; it acts direct on the seat of disease
and cures the cause, and then the effects of that
cause cease. Most other remedies offered are
prepared with a view of treating the symptoms,
consequently, as soon as the effect of the medicine
passes away, the trouble returns, and the patient
wants another bottle of medicine. This may be
very good for the maker of the remedy (?) but it is
not at all satisfactory to the patient. Clements
Tonic positively, effectually, and permanently
cures; it does not only relieve, like most of the
narcotic mixtures sold, but radically effects a cure ;
and if the patient only avoids the source from
which he or she contracted the illness in the first
place, a relapse is impossible. Moreover, Clements
Tonic does not constringe the gastric vessels of
the stomach or cause indigestion, and will not
injure nor discolor the teeth. Can any rival pre-
paration say the same?

Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of. a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
James Chambers, King-street, Sydney.
Society
The Sydney cabmen won’t call a shilling
“a bob” any more. They’ll call it “a Bishop
Smith.” Just you see.
* * *
Primate Smith’s predecessor of yore, Dr.
Barker, who went to Heaven long ago, was so
abnormally tall that (incited thereto also by the
fact that he was a teetotaller) the tavernites called
the “longest” drink of beer procurable at a
public-house “a Bishop Barker.” Better be the
godfather of a long drink than of a short fare.
# * *
Primate Smith says that he won t give a
cabman more than his fare, because to do so
would be unjust to poorer people. Poorer people !
Who should be poorer than a Christian bishop?

school prize-day speech : —“ Either in learnin’ or
in teachin’, people cannot be too careful about
speakin’ English correctly.” Our statesmen can’t
manage their aitohes. Our Governors don’t
manage their “g’s.”

Apropos a variety of things. It doesn’t
look well for a Minister to grant a pretty
civil servant a post far above the standard of
her certificate—especially if he and she happen
by the merest accident to live under the same
roof. Nor, when a newspaper offers a prize for a
literary effort, does it look well to see that prize
awarded to anyone within the “office ” circle.
* • *
At the Wilson-Churchill wedding in Eng-
land, Sir Salmon was surrounded by royalties and
half the British peerage. Happy man! He gave the
bride a marriage-present as vulgar as his own
flame-coloured whiskers : a gold toilet-set, “ which
included, besides the usual gold-stoppered bottles
and boxes, a gold basin and jug and a large gold-
framed mirror for the dressing-table.” The
cataloguer here pulls up suddenly.

Remark made for the benefit of anyone
whom it may concern. When once an editor
takes to wearing a revolver regularly, he must in
the long run shoot or be shot. The man who
presents a revolver signs his own death-warrant
if he misses or his enemy shoots first and straight.
Are we going to acclimatise the variety of
newspaper man ?

At last week’s meeting of the South Mel-
bourne (Larkin) Building Society one of the
depositors threw cold water on the hopes of a
payment in full by offering to take ten shillings in
the £ for his interest in the winding-up. A bad
look out for the shareholders, who don’t get a
“ look-in ” until depositors’ claims have been met.
* * #
The Sydney public wants a rest. It is
tired of having fired at it, in season and out of
season, in the social column of every newspaper
published in the city, the names of a pair of
people whose one object in life would appear to be
to attend every “ function ” and get an advertise-
ment out of it. The much-printed pair may be
“well-connected,” but neither of them is at all
interesting, if one overlooks the fact that in the
male can be seen the best and finest example of
vapid ignorance, brazen front and ghastly snob-
bery south of the equator.

The barn-dance trouble is, no doubt, at the bottom

The story goes that an amalgamation has
been effected between three Melbourne daily
papers, and that a Sydney man has made a
thumping commission out of the transaction.
# V * *
A writer in a Sunday contemporary,
deploring the hard lot of the Murrumbidgee
settlers, states that these latter are chiefly
Germans, and that their distress is so great that
women have to work on the farms. Wherever the
Germans are settled the women have to do quite
their share of “hard graft.” We have sat on a
fence and smoked with a fat German gentleman,
in the Wimraera district, while his wife was
ploughing in Wellington boots.
o # #
When Sydney Water and Sewerage Board
want to intimate to a citizen that he is expected
to pay some rates, they just send him a summons
carrying costs. The Board doesn’t believe in
preliminaries. It only believes in bailiffs. It is
true that if you remonstrate, the officials swear
that notice has been served, and cases have been
known in which dirty, trodden-on pieces of blue
paper bearing certain more or less legible inscrip-
tions have been found on office-floors, near the
front door. However, the Board is apparently
not bound to have the notices handed to anybody.
Its work is finished when it slips a bit of flimsy
under the mat at midnight.
# * *
The Rockhampton (Central Tophet) Hos-
pital Committee lately reduced the pay attaching
to the responsible office of resident surgeon (a post
very different from a city resident surgeonship)
from ,£750 to £GOO, and then to £3OO, wherefor
they are rated in no measured terms by Dr. J. A.
Langdon, who, however, remarks that, as there
are many impecunious medicoes seeking for gore
and the petty glories of an official position, the
ignorant and bumptious committee will have no
difficulty in finding a man to suit them.

Nothing like having two strings to your
bow. Sydney Evening News is just now profes-
sing strongly democratic tendencies. The Illus-
trated Sydney News (published from the same
office) is devoted to the glorification of wealth and
militarism, and is eternally printing gigantic
portraits of fat swashbucklers with weak chins
and with large feathers in their hats. It gives a
solid support to the N.S.W. “ National” Associa-
tion, established to dish the Labour-party !

The boss policeman down at South Mel-
bourne is said to be .£BOO the poorer by Larkin’s
embezzlements, so it seems probable that Matthias
hasn’t been getting so many indulgences in gaol,
after all. Yet “quod” is the most comfortable
place for him under the circumstances. There
are at least two old men prowling around with
firearms concealed about their persons, and stating
that as they haven’t long to live or any money
left to live with, they would be glad to get one
pop at their old pal, Matt., and then retire upon
the consequences.
# # •
The magnates of the N.S.W. civil service
managed to keep very quiet the story of the gen-
tleman (now in “ quod ”) who first forged his way
into the Lands Office, then transferred himself
per medium of a forgery to the Audit Office, and
finally (through one of those stupid blunders which
are made by even the sharpest men) forged his
way into gaol. To do the gaol authorities justice,
they were so afraid that he would forge his way
out of gaol that they kept him away from pens
and ink. Another N.S.W. Civil Service story
about which the public know little is that of the
gaol-official who, not long ago, was found em-
bezzling quite a lot of money and was simply
dismissed—for how can you punish a man who
steals in gaol ? Perhaps, however, a man can only
endure imprisonment in his own gaol.
» # *
Two sisters were committed for trial for
bigamy at one sitting of the Redfern (Sydney)
Police-court last week. One of them pleaded that
she had received from her husband —against whom
she held a protection-order—a letter (produced)
allowing her to marry whom she pleased. This
miserable woman has offended against the law,
but has, morally speaking, been guilty of no
crime. The woman who has to be protected
against her husband should be set free. If N.S.W.
had an equitable divorce-law she could have re-
married without risk of a bigamy prosecution.

Belle Bilton Clancarty lias supplied a
tender, romantic sequel to the Dunlo divorce-
case by presenting her reconciled husband with
twin boys. Seeing that the young man has
succeeded his pig-headed father and Belle wears
a “coronick” the twin that took the lead in this
little matter will be Lord Dunlo, always provided
that nurse doesn’t “ mix them babies up,' in
which case the last may go first without making
much difference to hereditary legislation. Mean-
while one would like to hear what Wertheimer
the lady’s former protector, afterwards the co-
respondent, and finally a friend of the noble
family, has to say about the noble event—
Death has entered into mimic life, with
a tragedy in the Dampier household at Mel-
bourne. Selina Palmer, the lady-help, slays her-
self with Rough on Rats, through unrequited, and
indeed undisclosed, affection for Alfred Tisch-
bauer, the Alexandra scenic artist. The most
terrible interposition of the real into the ideal
was when Federici died suddenly, as he descended
the trap as Mephistopheles, at the Princess’. A
pretty blonde on the Collins-street Block, now
getting rather ancient, had a strange experience
when engaged as a child in the pantomime ballet
a hoy, aged 14, committed suicide on her
account, Garrity, a lad of 15, Hung himself under
the train at Richmond (Melb.), and was killed,
through unrequited love, while another remark-
able Melbourne suicide (that of a little South Mel-
bourne boy) was by drowning, through love of
Ftmlie Melville, and this in her later days.
Fannie has been fatal at all ages to males of all
ages.
Bingham Crowther, the absconding Hobart
medico, was a regular proraenader on the Block
during his stay in Sydney. This foolhardiness is
thoroughly characteristic of the Crowtherian
character, in which prudence or diplomacy has no
place. “Bing.,” as ho is familiarly called by
Hobart folk, is a handsome, dashing fellow, like
his big brother Ted—also a medico, and an M.P.
to boot. In Sydney, as in Hobart, many a lass
craned her neck to catch a fuller glimpse of
Bing.’s bright features. With the exception of
shaving off his beard, Bing, hardly took any pre-
caution to conceal his identity from the dunder-
headed detectives of Sydney. For several days at
least he rubbed shoulders on the Block with the
police and the detectives. And but for the arrest
of Crowther at Colombo the writer would not
have chronicled this item for the edification of
Chief of Police Fosbery. The Tasmanians resem-
ble the Scotch very much in the canniness of their
disposition. Hence the non-revelation of Bing.’s
presence in Sydney. Several Hobartians on the
Sydney press could, if they chose, point out the
big hotel which Bing honoured with his patron-
age. But what surprises his countrymen in
Sydney is* that he didn’t sail out of Sydney in a
deep-sea boat.

to The Bulletin once, and that was to deny that

With regard to an article in a Sydney Sunday-
paper stating that the lunatic O’Farreii’s brother
was a clerk in the Victorian Lands Department
who embezzled a lot of money, the statement is
incorrect. Hugh James O’Ferrell, the clerk
referred to, was a West Indian with dark blood in
his veins, and in no way related to O’Farrell. Nor
did O’Farreii’s sister marry a certain celebrated
V ictorian cricketer, whose name it is unnecessary
to mention here; that cricketer’s wife is the
daughter of a well-known Western district settler.

In one street at Redfern (Sydney) are two
adjoining boarding-houses—one occupied by seven
bookmakers, the other by fourteen policemen.

One of the thrilling features of prison-life
in a certain Australian province is to see a judge’s
wife dropping in affably at the gaol to exchange
small talk with a very swell prisoner, a private
room being set apart for their meeting so that
they may not be disturbed. And another thrilling
feature is to see the distracted wife of a poor and
unfashionable prisoner applying for permission to
see her husband—who is in thesame gaol for ex-
actly the same offence as the criminal dude afore-
mentioned —and being told in a hard, brass-toned
voice at the gate that it is “agin’ the regula-
shuns,” and that she can’t be allowed in. If this
Most noble Clanoarty, you fortunate party,
You lucky, twice lucky, young Earl!
No Bacon or Milton his hopes could have built on
A wiser foundation of girl.
When Belle you were ringing, remarks very stinging
Were made, and men called you a dunce ;
There’s virtue, my hid, in the proof of the puddin’—
A pair of fine babies at once.
No snapping or snarling, henceforth, at your darling,
No mention of suits in divorce,
For Belle is the mother of “ Dun.” and his brother,
And you are the father, of course.
’Twas well that reviling produced reconciling,
A mutual forgiveness of sins :
For if, just consider, she’d stayed a grass widder
You wouldn’t be nursing the twins.
AT THE “J.C.” (WEST QUEENSLAND
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
None ever knew his name,
Honoured, or one of shame,
Highborn, or lowly ;
Only, upon that tree
Two letters, J and C,
Carved by him, mark where he
Lay dying slowly.
Why came he to the West?
Had then the parent nest
Grown so distasteful ?
What cause had he to shun
Life, ere ’twas well begun ?
Was he that youngest son
Of substance wasteful ?
Were fate and he at war?
Was it a penance, or
Renunciation ?
Is it a glad release ?
Has he at length found peace
Now Death had bid him cease
Peregrination?
Hands white, without a blot,
Told us that he was not
One of “ the vulgar.”
What can those cypheis be?
Two only, J and 0,
Carved in his agony,
Deep in the “Mulga ?”
Was there no woman’s face
Whose sunny smile might chase
Clouds from above him ?
No bosom white as snow ?
No lips to whisper low,
“ Why doth he seek to go?
Do 1 not love him ? ”
Haunted by flashing charms,
White bosoms, rounded arms,
Lips of fair ladies,
Striving to break some link,
’Twas that which made him sink,
Dragged by the curse of drink
Deeper than Hades?
Now, wind across the grave
Tuning a sultry stave,
Drearily whistles,
Stirring those branches, where
Two silent cyphers stare,
Two letters of a prayer,
God’s Son’s initials.
N.S. Wales.

* * *
An elderly, much -married Melbourne
gentleman —one of those unctuous heavy fathers
who like to find some nice-looking girl and be a
parent to her—has come to grief badly. He came
across a lovely girl in a theatre who looked as if
she wanted some protecting arm to temper the wind
to the shorn maiden in short skirts, and he offered
to find her a home, - with the society of a moral
elderly person thrown in. She accepted the home
and he furnished it magnificently, but when he
came round the very first evening after his angei
took possession a quiet-looking man opened the
door and asked him, as a favour, not to call again.
His wife, he said blandly, was tired after her
work at the theatre, and moreover they lived a
very retired life and seldom cared to see strangers
—here the old stout man leaned against the door-
post and breathed stertorously. Moreover, the
children took up all her spare time—the fat pro-
tector sat down on the doorstep at this point and
asked himself if this was all a horrid dream. Then
the door closed softly, and the ancient and moral
gentleman got up and crawled away with an
awful conviction that he had been beguiled into
supplying a house and furniture to a plain, hard-
working family with 11 children, and that for all
the romance there was in the affair he might as
well have gone and embraced an elderly gamp
who washed floors for a livelihood. And the
moral is that youth and loveliness and romance
don’t always go together, and that an innocent
and affectionate soul is more often hidden behind
a watery green eye and an old, squashed, brown
bonnet than behind a halo of curls of gold.
# * *
It has just been decided in England that
no man is compelled to stay in a church against
his will, and that he must not be detained there
by violence. A man went into a cathedral the
other day in that blessed land of the free, and sat
patiently there till the sermon came on. Then
the preacher gave out his text, and droned away
till the sufferer could stand it no longer and started
for the door. But he was arrested on the way by
the beadle or somebody, and informed that he
couldn’t quit the sacred edifice unless he was ill.
The beadle was the bigger man of the two, and he
had to stay in the sacred edifice. But when he
got out he took action for assault and battery,
libel, illegal detention in a Cathedral, and demur-
rage, and the court decided that it is the privilege
of every free-born Englishman to quit a cathedral
when he wants to.

correspondent couldn’t have added —“ Fortunately
Mr. Morgan and the cottage were both insured.”

A reporter rushed breathlessly into Sydney D.T. office the other day with this
startling item“ A cow was struck dead in a yard at the back of Cobar during a heavy
thunderstorm which broke over the town a few days ago.”
With Professor Tigwissel’s patent back-action, self-adjusting, non-forfeiting, time-
payment lightning-rods deplorable accidents of that sort need never happen.— Advt.
Christian can find him. The heathen can always
be found without a flag. And just here we would
suggest that the Christian might as well get over
such difficulties as this by wearing a distinctive
costume, like’ the bishop, or the butcher, or the
policeman. Something light and airy, such as a

and then, when a number of men were seen
wobbling drearily on a dusty shore and singing
Sankey’s hymns all through a blazing summer

• # *
The present period of big failures and
small assets is suitable for telling the old yarn
about one Throgmorton, a Melbourne importer
who came a fearful cropper in days of yore. The
creditors of Throgmorton met at his request to
consider what had better be done with his estate,
set down on the schedule at a matter of £9
sterling. They rolled up in a compact body,
armed with invoices, promissory-notes, and bills
payable to the amount of a few thousands in all,
and called upon insolvent to make a proposal.
He shrugged his shoulders and replied that he
was entirely in the hands of his kind friends in
front—how did they propose to deal with the
assets? Then the creditors turned a sad, re-
proachful gaze upon the last speaker (who had
been more or less of a “pal” with the whole
crowd) and suggested that there were no assets to
dispose of. Whereupon Mr. T. produced nine
sovereigns from his pocket and moved that the
golden remnants of his estate be spent in cham-
pagne, debtor and creditors to share and share
alike. The champagne was fetched in and the
meeting sang “He's a jolly good fellow . as
Throgmorton apparently was. He dug them in
their respective lots of ribs to such an extent that
they undertook to give him a fresh start on
“ tick,” after which this quaint insolvent went on
and prospered until he was able to wipe off the
old score with interest added. We get none or
such fine, dashing, handsome, romantic failures
nowadays. The modern bankrupt lies like a
tombstone to his creditors, and the blaspheming
creditors, knowing themselves to be shamefully
robbed, seek the aid of a trustee, who gets back
what he can from the first robber and generally
sticks to it himself.

The daughter of a wealthy Melbourne
medico who jilted a squatter to marry a foreigner,
supposed to be enormously wealthy, has met with
the usual fate. The foreigner, who hadn’t a
penny, has vanished.
# # #
Newcastle, N.S.W., has no gaol but it has
a court of Quarter Sessions, established to satisfy
the local storekeepers. Prisoners for trial are
brought by rail from Maitland, marched along
the streets —handcuffed in pairs—to the court-
house, whence they return in similar fashion,
followed by all the tag-rag-and-bobtail of the
town.
* * «
In an English divorce case the co-res-
pondents include a father and son. In an
Irish ease set down for hearing at Cork there are
20 co-respondents.
# * *
A Southern writer (a stock-and-station
agent, by the way) says that the pastoral people
eat three times as much beef or mutton as town
residents ; 2|lb. daily is not high for them. On
sheep stations a man eats IJlb. chops for breakfast.
Seven residents will get through each week 1501 b.
of sheep. The like number on a cattle station get
through a 6001 b. beast easily in a month, though
the meat at last becomes hard and salt.
A STRIKE STORY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ Flash ” Harry stood and scratched his ear,
They called him so, their reason
I’ll demonstrate and make as clear
As ’tis that Christmas comes each year,
When eggs are out of season.
In three weeks’ time he was to wed
A back-block belle named Mary,
W r ith her the path of life to tread—
A pleasant prospect, yet it shed
No light on his quandary.
Imagine now, how you would like
Stern fate to torture you so,
The tailor-men went out on strike,
Got the accentuated spike,
And wouldn’t make his trousseau.
“ I’d make a tailor’s head feel sore
If I could only cop one ;
An outfit ready made, oh, lor 1
The like I never heard before,
To have to get a slop one !
The only thing that I can do,
Though doing that with loathing,
Will be to send my measure to
That Sydney firm (we’ll call it Q)
Who make this ‘Austral’ clothing.”
He got a friend to run the show,
Who once had been a draper,
Who measured him from top to toe,
And round the body, high and low,
And put it down on paper.
He got a cheque for pounds and pence,
All duly signed and dated,
And had it crossed, to show his sense,
And then, for fresh developments
He sat him down and waited.
The long weeks vanished like a shot,
The fatal day drew nigher,
My hero’s speech grew strong and hot,
Because, you see, he hadn’t got.
His marrying attire.
’Twas not until the fatal morn
That he received the shipment,
A parcel (with its wrapper torn)
Of clothes, his figure to adorn,
His hymen’al equipment.
He quickly closed his chamber-door,
And donned them, with elation,
But Heavens ! how my hero swore,
Fit to unhinge his lower jaw,
With grief and horror, when he saw,
That slop-shop perpetration.
When in the glass his image caught,
His words both sharp and terse were.
Not fit for use in open courts
Because the coat was far too short,
The trousers the reverse were.
And where they should have clung most tight
Their slackness was tremendous,
In fact, he was a woeful sight,
From falling into such a plight
May heaven now defend us.
Yet he took comfort, and said he,
“This grievous visitation
Though bad enough, might yet worse be,
Perchance its incongruity
Is all imagination.”
Stifling his fears by force of will,
The subject of this lyric
Sped to the church to seek his “ Jin,’’
Who, with vituperation shrill
Opened flood-gates hysteric.
“Marry him in such awful clothes,
Just Heaven ! what effront’ry !”
And while the tears bedecked her nose
She voiced the burden of her woes,
Across the western country.
And so a compromise was made,
They tuned the harp and psalter
(I mean the loud harmonium played! !
“ Flash ” Harry led the blushing maid
All smiling to the altar.
Believe me ’twas a touching sight,
And one to make your pulse stir,
For, though the sun shone hot and bright,
My hero faced the wedding-rite,
Attirfed in an ulster.
SURCINGLE.
On Guard.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Paddy Boyle was licensed to sell fermented
and spirituous liquors at the Mulga Hotel, on the
Dromedary Road, Sandfly district, Bananaland.
As a matter of fact, he sold rum—and rum only.
Paddy Boyle hadn’t too much custom, but he
made the most of what chances he did get. The
horses of travellers —more especially of moneyed
travellers —seemed to have a rooted objection to
the grass that grew in the immediate vicinity of
the ridge on the summit of which sweltered the
Mulga Hotel, with its perennial rum-cask, for
they were continually straying away and getting
lost, and had invariably to be trailed down by
Paddy’s black-boy, who as invariably used to
discover them just about the time when there was
no more corn in Egypt, and no more “ile ” in the
cruse, for their owner or owners. In fact, the
black-boy never found a man’s horse while that
man had any money left.
* * *
Sundown was just crisping the overdone
edges of a fierce summer’s day, when a four-horse
team toiled up the hill, driven by a hard-looking
man and followed by a harder-looking dog—both
with their tongues out. Unhitching his team,
and hobbling them out, the man made inside with
speed, and said —“Give us a bottle of beer, boss;
Pm as dry as a roasted snake.”
Paddy emptied out five-fingers of rum, with the
remark : “ That’s betther nor beer ; that’s the raal
Maokay. ”
The teamster swallowed it down, sizzlingly, and
smiled approval.
“ ’Nother,” said he, laconically.
Paddy responded with good-fellowship. The
smile on the teamster’s face merged into an
expression of imbecile vacancy—which is, to be
accurate, a want of expression. He curled down
on the verandah floor, and was presently dead to
the world.
Paddy immediately prospected the prostrate
form. It panned out well. Placing the pocket-
book and bundle back in the teamster’s pouch,
Paddy solemnly winked at the rum-bottle that
was still on the rough bar-counter, and said aloud:
“Shure, there do be no good feed round about
here for thim poor bastes; Oi’ll take ’em down
into the scrub pocket beyant, where they can fill
their bellies in no time wid big mouthfuls of swate
grane grass. ”
Off started Paddy on his merciful errand. As
he stooped to take the hobbles off the first horse a
live catapult hurled itself upon his back, threw
him down violently, swallowed about half of his
shirt, and then lay down beside him on most
malignant guard.
It was the teamster’s dog.
That ill-favoured animal was chums with the
horses and had acquired the habit of tending them
every night.
Paddy made a few attempts to coax the canine,
but failed utterly. No sooner did he move a
limb, too, than his gaoler rumbled forth a heart-
chilling growl, and once or twice made a spring of
menace. Paddy had perforce to lie still, while
mosquitoes and sandflies ate him up and a city of
ants held a racing carnival on his anatomy. So
passed a long and agonising night.
# # #
Early in the night, the teamster awoke on
the verandah, and crawled indoors for a corpse-
reviver. Mrs. Boyle was on duty, not knowing,
and apparently not caring, where Paddy was.
The visitor seemed a trifle shy of the same old
bottle at first, but, as Mrs. B. helped herself out
of it, he took courage and swigged it down man-
fully.
Then he had another. Mrs, B. had another.
The black-boy came in and was treated to “a
snifter.”
After which the teamster and Mrs. B. had 38
consecutive drinks.
At this stage, the black-boy commenced to help
himself and ask no questions.
After that —vermilion chaos in full undress.
# # *
The teamster and the black-boy chopped
away the verandah-posts, and the roof came down
with a run. Upon which, the teamster threw the
black-boy through the window and completely
wrecked the front of the shanty. Whereupon,
Mrs. B. began to bombard the teamster with pro-
miscuous bottles, and smashed the only other
window, and knocked sieve-holes into everything
but the man she aimed at. Then she jumped on
the counter and yelled, “Eire!” and wept aloud for
her native country, which was Ireland. The team-
ster, about this time, began to remember his old
digging-days, and, under the notion thatthe ground
beneath the Mulga Hotel was worth prospecting,
he started preliminary work by tearing up the
verandah flooring, and starting a fire underneath
his dray. Summoning the nigger to aid, he
carried out all the moveable furniture and piled
that also on the fire, while Mrs. B. danced jigs in
the most joyous style, she herself doing her own
lilting, and calling on the absent Paddy to come
and be murthered at wanst. At last the teamster
climbed on to the top of a 400-gallon ship’s tank,
and fell asleep with his long legs dangling over the
sides and attracting the up-curling flames. Mrs.
Boyle went off and bailed herself up in the cow-
bail, under the impression that it was milking-
time. She shortly fell prone and asleep. The
black-boy had sense enough to collar the last half-
keg of rum and roll it down-hill to his own camp,
where fragments of it were found next day. This
was after it had caught fire and exploded.
* • #
Towards morning, the hawker, turning in
his sleep, rolled off the tank, and came to grass
with a thud that scared ten years’ growth out of
him. As he gazed upon the wreck around him,
and heard the also-awakened Mrs. Boyle yelling
for somebody to come and unbail her, he realised
that it was time to go and look for his horses.
He found them.
He also found Paddy, who had been a helpless,
prisoner-witness of the night’s pandemonium.
“Call off this (incarnadine) devil of a dahg !”
said Paddy, furiously, “ an’ Oi’ll smash ivery
bone in yer ugly carcase to smithereens.”
The teamster was fightable, and the pair
clinched, while the dog acted as umpire to see
fair-play—for his master. Memory loses the rest
in a confused kaleidoscope of curses, mud, gore,
woman, scorched nigger, stampeding horses, man-
eating dog, utter exhaustion, discovery of grog
famine, and a( wild hunt after the flying remains
of the half-grilled black-boy.

QUEENSLAND.
, NOTICE.
Sale by Auction of Grown Lands Tn Large
Portions.
IT is notified for public information that, under
the provisions of “ The Special Sales of Land
Apt of 1891,” Country Lands in the Colony of
Queensland, distant not less than twenty miles
from a navigable river, or from a railway, or from
the surveyed line of a railway for the construction
of which money has been raised by loan, may be
offered for Sale by Auction, in portions not exceed-
ing 5120 acres. The-upset price will in no case be
less than 10s. per acre.
The time for payment may be extended to a
period not exceeding three years, instalments
payable later than six months from the date of
sale bearing interest at 5 per cent, per annum.
Persons desirous of having Crown lands offered
at auction are invited to apply to the Under-
secretary for Public Lands, Brisbane, or any
Crown Lands Commissioner.
Department of Public Lands,
Brisbane, 28th October, 1891.
Tasmania. Messrs, vi t z gerald

Shanty,” Price, One Shilling; Postage 3d. extra.
A. S. COWLEY,

Editor of Nahbethong (Vic.) Gazette :
“ Would our friends kindly excuse any short-
comings at this festive season, inasmuch as we are
our own editor, sub, reporter, canvasser, composi-
tor, and reader.” Editor of Narbethong Inde-

From Lippincott.s, wherein a description
of a tiffin among the Mongols is given ;—The
Chinese nobility retire regularly to a vomitoriurn
in order to make room for the next instalments of
the feast. The plates are piled full of food every
two minutes, and the courses vary from 50 to
200!
Whalley, the godly lawyer who has just
died at Blackburn, Eng., leaving behind him the
usual shortage, kept a staff of fourteen clerks in
his two establishments. Ho was supposed to be
rich, and prided himself on his reputation for
piety and benevolence. His charities are described
as numerous. Ho is spoken of as a great reader
of religious literature, which he ostentatiously
exhibited in his office. At his office, Whalley had
a room which he used periodically as an oratory,
and into which lie .daily retired for private prayer.
On the pages of a memorandum which was found
in one of his drawers are crosses at intervals,
with the words, in his own handwriting, “ Pray
fervently.” A client relates that he was shown
the oratory, and told by Whalley “that he had
passed many happy hours in that little room in
silent communion with his Maker ” —to whom lie
went leaving behind him bogus deeds instead of
mortgages on real property, and valueless LO.U.’s
in the place of marketable seouritus.

all through your “pome.” Westbrook” (Tas.):

only make us feel tired. We would have to go Tight

WHAT EVERYBODY SHOULD KNOW.
Sprains are injuries done to the ligaments,
tendons, and other structures surrounding the
joints. They are produced by jumping, falling,
lifting heavy weights, or other causes, which
forcibly stretch or lacerate the tendons, &c. Ihe
joints most liable to sprains are the wrist ami
ankle joints. The latter are more frequently
sprained by persons in the act of jumping or
running, or by the foot turning under the weight
of the body, while the wrist may be sprained in a
fall, or by handling heavy objects, and so on.
Injuries of this kind are of the most common
occurrence, and everyone should know how to best
treat them. Sprains are attended with great
pain at the time of the accident, and also with
considerable swelling and discoloration of the
injured part; the swelling and discoloration arise
irom the effusion of blood into the surrounding
structures. In the treatment of sprains the
injured limb must be kept in a perfect state of
quietude, and in such a position as to relax the
muscles in connection with the affected tendons,
as well as to favor the return of the blood to the
heart. The bowels should be kept in a free state
and hot fomentations applied to the sprain by
means of a sponge or piece of thick flannel. Next,
dry gently with a soft Turkish towel, and im-
mediately after apply St. Jacobs Oil, which
should be rubbed, as the patient may be able to
i bear, over and around the part affected, with the
palm of the hand ; then bandage tightly with
; strips of old linen saturated in the same liniment,
and let the operation be repeated at intervals
; until a cure is effected. By this treatment the
disease will rapidly subside, and the affected part
will return to its usual healthy condition. r llns
was the experience of Mr. James Grant, of No. 2,
j Kent-street, Sydney, who writes: “I formerly
; worked on the Margaret-street wharf, and while
there the sinews of my forearm gave way, and I
, was unable to lift anything, and so prevented
I from following my employment. I was advised
; to try St. Jacobs Oil, and did so with good effect,
as my arms are now quite strong, and I can carry 7
heavy weights without feeling anything of my
former complaint.” Cut this out and paste it in

HOROLOGIOAL CURIOSITIES.
A BLIND MAN’S WATCH.
Her Majesty Queen Victoria has two beautiful
little gold watches, by Breguet, that are
supposed to be 100 years old. They have silver
dials and are about the size of a two shilling piece.
One is a blind man’s watch; the other is a
repeater. Both go perfectly, and are in constant
u«e The Queen’s favorite watch is a large plain
gold one by Mudge, the English maker. The
favorite watches with persons of lesser degree are
those known as the Waterburys. These ate
manufactured in a variety of styles, and are sold
at prices which, taking into consideration their
finish and excellency as timekeepers, must be re-
garded as nothing short of phenomenal, ihe
great success which has attended the sale of these
watches, and the almost unanimous verdict of the
public in their favor, conclusively prove the claims
made for the Waterburys. .
An important fact in connection with these
marvellous watches is their ease and cheapness of
repair. In the event of injury befalling one of
them, on taking or sending it to the Waterbury
Watch Co.’s Repair Depot, 56, Margaret-street,
Sydney, it will be efficiently repaired at a merely
nominal cost. Note this for reference.
