In a recent rabbit-drive at Cobar (N.S. W.)
50,000 were killed in one paddock and 80,000 in
another. Runs which lately carried 50,000 sheep
are now reduced to 7000, for which food has to be
cut. In the Wagga district the biggest haul of
rabbits was 33,318 (on Billingenambil) in three
yardings.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
At latest advices the interminable Osman
Digna was preparing to start once more that war
which never quite ends in the Soudan. This is
another nasty reflection on the Australian Con-
tingent. That famous circus went to the Soudan,
wandered about for two or three months with a
brass band and a flag, and then came home with
a stolen donkey and an annexed goat and called it
war. Lut it wasn’t war, for the one great essen-
tial in a campaign is that each side must be pre-
pared to see the affair through to the bitter end,
and the army that is liable to get tired and go
back to its mother is a failure. Osman is evi-
dently prepared to hang out in Africa for 40
yearn, and if persistence counts for anything he
will finish by leading his “dervishes” into London
and turning St. Paul’s into a mosque.
THE PHINCE OF WALES SPEAKS ON
HEALTH.
In his able and timely remarks at the opening
of the International Congress of Hygiene, at St.
James’s Hall, London, August 10th, 1891, the
President, the Prince of Wales, said :—“ The
highest possible prosperity is when rich and poor
a'xke can do such useful work as then are lit for ,
and to this end it is essential that they' should enjoy
the best possible health and vigor ,
Very true, but how can the public health bo
best promoted ‘I Clearly by such preventive
measures as the Congress meets to consider, and
by reliable remedies so long as certain diseases
continue to prevail.
We submit an example of the latter necessity in
the form of a statement which cannot fail to con-
vince the minds of all who may read it.
[copy.]
I, Margaret Morland, of Throstle Nest Farm,
Haya Park, near Knaresboro’, Yorkshire, do
solemnly and sincerely declare as follows :—
“I have suffered all my life from a weak
stomach, indigestion and liver complaint. 1 wag
never properly well but always ailing attd neVel
seemed to gain strength. I had a bad taste ih the
mouth and a strange sinking feeling at the pit of
my stomach. I had always pain after eating, no
matter how simple the food was. My appetite
was poor and what little I ate did not seem to
give me strength. I had great pain at the chest
and sides and felt as if I was held or bound round
my waist. 1 was much troubled with sickness
and very often found no ease until I vomited all
the food I had taken. I felt low, weak, and de-
pressed, as if something was hanging over me. 1
could not sleep at night, and got so bad that I
even dreaded going to bed, for I would lie awake
for hours together, and on rising in the morning I
felt more tired than when I went to bed. As
years went on I became a bad colour, my skin and
particularly the whites of toy eyes were tinged of
a yellowish colour, and at times 1 was as yellow
as a sovereign. In August, 1880, my stomach
became very irritable and everything that I took
turned sour on my stomach, and I was constantly
vomiting a foul bitter fluid or gas. I took little
or no nourishment, and after each morsel of even
liquid, food I strained and vomited as if my
poor inside would come out. The pain at
my heart now became so severe that I be-
came alarmed and sent for our doctor, who
attended me for ten or twelve weeks, but all the
medicine he gave me did no good. The doctor
seemed puzzled with my complaint and changed
the medicine over and over again, but still I got
worse instead of better. At last I lost all faith in
physic and gave up taking it. Now better, now
worse, I continued until July, 1888, when my
kidneys became affected. I had dreadful pain in
the back and could not void my water. My skin
was dry and hot, my lips parched and feverish.
After a time the water lodged so bad that I
thought I should have died. I grew weaker and
weaker every day, and felt that if a change did not
soon take place my condition would be critical, for
the pain was more than I could bear. I could not
even bide to lie in bed, and had to get out of bed and
walk about the room. I took all kinds ofmedi
nines, but nothing gave me more than temporary
relief, and I dragged on in this miserable stale
until December, 1888, when Mr. Day, the chemist,
at Knaresboro’, sent me a book telling of a medi-
cine called Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and J
read of a case similar to mine having been cured
by this medicine. I determined to try it. and
sent to Mr. Day and procured a large buttle of the
Syrup and commenced taking it. 1 soon began to
find relief, my food digested better, and gave me
strength, all pains in my back and sides left me.
I could now sleep better and gradually gained
strength every day, and by the time I had taken
a 4s. 6d. bottle 1 was better than ever 1 was in my
life, and by taking an occasional dose I have since
kept in good health. 1 may mention that I paid
over six pounds to one doctor and did not receive
any benefit whatever. I feel very grateful for
the great benefit I have received from Seigel's
Syrup, and I wish others to know of it. I have
recommended the medicine to many people in tliis
district with great benefit. My daughter, Mrs.
Ware, living at York, was cured of severe dys-
pepsia after every other means had failed. If by
publishing this statement others may be benefited
I shall be very glad, and I hereby give permission
to the proprietors of Seigel’s Syrup to make any
use they may think fit of this testimonial, and I
make this solemn declaration conscientiously be-
lieving the same to be true. By virtue of the
Provisions of the Statutory Declaration Act, 1835
(William IV. c, 62).
Declared before me at Leeds,'
in the County of York, by
the said Margaret Morland,
on Monday, the 10th of -
August, 1891.
(Signed) Alf. Cooke,
Mayor of Leeds. ,
(Signed)
Margaret Morland.
Mr. William Mori and, the husband of this lady,
is a fanner, well-known and highly respected. He
lias lived on his present farm at Throstle Nest,
Haya Park, seventeen years, and in the district
all his life. He was present at Leeds when the
foregoing declaration by his wife was read, and
vouched for its accuracy and truth. Mrs. Mor-
land’s malady, indigestion and dyspepsia, is suf-
ficiently common and fatal to make it a duty to
give the widest publicity to any remedy that
will cure it. Hence our mention of the above
ease in connection with the work of the society of
which His Royal Highness is President.
Macgregor Land.
The long and elaborate reports Sir Wil-
I lam-Maegregor writes about British New Guinea
are all padding. It is absolute heresy to say this,
ut course just as it’s downright blasphemy to say
that Stanley’s “original ” African nigger-stories
are scissored out of Uncle Remus’ book. Macgregor
has, ime Buli.ktin is credibly informed, never
been 50 miles from the coast, except by water.
. 18 own reports are noticeably remarkable for his
fondness for getting into the interior per river
way. Now, any man with two good legs can
walk up Mount Owen Stanley by simply sending
a party ahead to cut a track. Certainly, Mac-
gregor is always on the move, and fussing about
to make the natives understand his laws, while he
autocratically shuts out white men from examin-
ing or opening up the country, under the plea of
philanthropy. For being on the move, Macgregor
gets A 2 a day as ex’s. ; and his philanthropy
ensures a lasting Government by Administration.
It is, however, useless for officialdom to attempt
to spoon back the tidal wave of progress, and
British New as a part of Australia, has
got to come out of the shell of Crown Administra-
tion and move up. Already German New Guinea is
being opened up rapidly, while British New
Guinea remains choked by officialdom. As it is
attached to Queensland, the Government of that
province has a right to exercise some direct con-
trol over the New Guinea territory. At present
the sum of <£16,000 per annum paid by the Aus-
tralian provinces to run New Guinea is absolutely
wasted. The place might fairly be called Mac-
gregor Land.
* * *
Macgregor’s judicature is nob a startling
success. Half of it is misunderstood by the
natives; the other half is ridiculed by them —when
they dare. Botany Bay in miniature would not
be a bad name for it. Already it has a chain-
gang working on the roads, and there have been
hangings. If a native steals the pig of another—
and is caught— to the chain-gang he goes. At the
first hanging festival, Macgregor naturally needed
gallows. It is related that he showed the
Government carpenter (!) a plan of the hanging
apparatus in vogue at Newgate, and wanted a
similar convenience erected at Port Moresby!
Ihe carpenter puzzled his brains over the plans,
and finally reported that such machinery would
be too elaborate and expensive for hanging an
odd black or two, and recommended that two
upright poles and a cross-beam might be an im-
provement upon Newgate advanced thought.
Unit suggestion, fortunately for Macgregor’s
reputation for sanity, was adopted.
• * *
Officialdom is a happy family at Port
Moresby, but it swaggers much, with, per-
haps, the exception of Colonial Secretary
Musgrave, who seems intelligent and capable,
and who is obliging. Whether the others are
intelligent or not, the writer was unable to learn.
One of Macgregor’s beliefs is that he is an ex-
plorer, as his previous Pacific records painfully
o jtt'ude upon their reader, and Colonial Treasurer
aptam Hatton-Richards (as if he hadn’t been
sufficient]}''christened, “the boys” give him ever
so many other sobriquets of esteem) well,
Hatton-Richards has been unkind enough to say
that Macgregor was “an explorer second only
to Stanley ! At one native village, the natives
call their prize-boar, “ The Governor !” This, as
a compliment.
* * *
lo wander afield. A German steamer
was going up the St. Augusta River, German
N.G. , and landed a crew at a village. A photo-
grapher was aboard and ho landed also, with a
view of “taking” a group of natives. After
immense trouble and many failures, he got the
ll, &f>ers to stand still, and, proceeding to the
camera, put his head under the cloth—whizz' a
shower of spears! Again he gift the duskies
quiet, and again—spears! He gave it best.

Head —‘ A Golden Shanty.’
Let Us be Careful.
Dear Bulletin, —Let us be careful. Let us
be very careful. The defenders of our peace
are abroad, and in the enthusiasm of then-
ideas of defending it, we may find our-
selves one of these salubrious mornings in
the lock-up. We have great respect, amounting
almost to awe, of the “Fooroe.” It contains a
fine body of men —as an undertaker would say.
Nevertheless, we are going—with fear and tremb-
ling—to take liberties with it.
In the first instance, we say that no carpenter,
bricklayer, mason, or ordinary tradesman could
afford to own a terrace of houses out of his legiti-
mate savings. Yet nearly every policeman who
retires can do this. The terrace of houses has
been a gibe against policemen for a long time.
There is more than sarcasm in it. The policeman is
only human —which seems almost blasphemy—
and no surer of the result of his speculations than
the ordinary human being. He must lose at times;
but he always comes out right with his little ter-
race to collect rents from at the end of his time.
Sir Robert Peel, who instituted him, must at the
same time have arranged with Providence a special
miracle 6n the policeman’s behalf to the purpose
that he should pretty nearly always end his days
in affluence.
It is a curious thing, by the way, that on look-
ing through the book which is kept in every
police-office the entries concerning the amount of
money found on “drunks” rarely exceed a
shilling or two, and a pipe. Sometimes they run
to three-and-sixpenoe and a pocket-knife, but that
may be in the case of a town councillor.
“Close the pubs.,” said a retired policeman
to us one day when, in a moment of sar-
donic humour, we made a suggestion that
doing so would be a good thing, “ Close the pubs. !
Then if you do,” he said, bringing his fist down
on his own bar-counter with a crash that made all
the glasses rattle —“My boy will never jine the
foorce.” Of course not. No drunken men—no
terraces.
We begin to be afraid that in the preceding
remarks we have not been as careful as we had in-
tended to be. We are not terribly troubled, for
all that, when we come to think of it, There was
an old Roman question concerning the Praetorian
Guards “ Quis custodiet custodes !” which
applies considerably to our defenders of the peace.
The majority of them are young Irishmen aching
for a row. The Irish are supposed to be the
monopolists of bulls, but surely John Bull deserves
his title when he made Irishmen his “defenders
of the peace.” Donnybrook Fair is the ideal of
most of them as a harmless and healthy recrea-
tion. “Fwhy,” said a constable to us one
right on Princes Bridge, Melbourne, “ if it wasn’t
for the fightin’ and skylarkin’ I’d lave the fooroe
to-morrow.”
We have put these things somewhat too flip-
pantly perhaps, but they lead to one effect. An
irascible young constable, in the full-blown glory
of being able to order people to “move on,” and
such like, comes across an inoffensive citizen
(probably standing at the corner waiting for a
friend) who objects to being “ moved on ” in such
an ignominious way. An altercation ensues and
the inoffensive citizen is hauled to the lock-up.
We have an instance in our memory of this same
thing being done in the city of Melbourne to a
magistrate. Of course the constable was a new
constable, and. of course, he apologised and, of
course, he was severely reprimanded. ‘ But all
people are not magistrates.
There is a thing we wish to put plainly before
those who are supposed to make laws. A vulgar
and illiterate constable in course of time becomes
a vulgar, illiterate, and ignorantly arrogant ser-
geant. This man, in the way of routine, learns
the procedure of police-courts. Justices of the
Peace are appointed, not on account of then-
knowledge, intellect, or known wisdom, but
merely on account of their position. Mr. Brown
is an eminent brick-contractor. Mr. Smith is an
equally eminent shoe-vendor. An intelligent
Government sees at once that they have all the
qualifications necessary for Justices of the
Peace. Ask one of them about the Laws of
Justinian—he will probably want to commit you
for contempt of Court. Ask him about anything
connected with our own chaotic code of law, and
he will—well he will know nothing and know
it insolently. The natural result is that the
ignorant and arrogant sergeant who has learnt the
procedure—not the real spirit and theory of the
law, remember, and let us further say not being
capable of understanding it—has this ignorant and
almost equally arrogant Bench at his fingers’ ends,
as the phrase runs. “Do ye plade guilty?” said
an eminent bricklaying J. P., in Carlton, once to
a prisoner who was accused of making a row of
some sort. “No, your Worship,” replied the pri-
soner. “But ye are,” said the magistrate. “No,”
answered the prisoner firmly. “Take him
away,” howled the dispenser of justice, “seven
days’for contempt of court.” And in an under
tone to his fellows on the bench—he was chair-
man—“l’m d dif I’m go’in to let a fellow
like that call me a liar. ”
The following incident is calculated to charm
those who believe in the Spartan integrity of the
police. Not long ago a man—a respectable and
sober man—was locked up, dragged to gaol,
because—a policeman had struck him! These are
the simple facts : He was standing quietly at the
side of the street when a policeman came up and
called him most of the names in the slang dic-
tionary, and then struck him. The man, even
then, mildly expostulated—and was handcuffed.
In the cell he was assaulted by the same policeman !
These facts came out in Court, but what said
the Magistrate ? * ‘ The police must be protected
even when they exceed their duty ; 40s. or seven
days !” Of course they must be protected. Poor
things ! If they were not protected where would
their terraces be ? But this is a frivolous question.
—A Spalpeen.
r“A Spalpeen” probably does the force a grave in*
justice, but it is just as well he should be permitted to
have his little jibe. His attempted ventilation of this
long-discussed question gives us an opportunity of re-
marking that policemen usually accumulate money
because the discipline to which they are subjected makes
them steady and sober, because they enjoy fair pay and
get it regularly, and because they have plenty of time to
scheme. We shall be glad to receive any new theories on
this debateable question.—Ed. B.]

SPORTING NOTIONS.
But for Sharpe getting his “hand in ” shortly
after 3 p.m on Jan. 1, the Australians might have knocked
up a long, slow score against the Sheffield Lambs in their
first innings. Attewell, the Britishers’ main hope on a
dry wicket, didn’t look dangerous at any time, ditto Leh-
mann and Briggs. Peel, too, was losing all his terrors for
them when Sharpe was put on for the second or third try
and began to be as deadly as the “demon ” at his best.
The halls that howled Bruce and Donnan were, humanly
speaking, unplayable. Satan might have cut them lo the
boundary, and pulled ’em cleverly to square leg, but a
weak mortal seldom gets his bat on such scorchers, and
when he does contrive to touch them as they fly upward
to his hails there are five men in the slips waiting for a
catch. Lohmann’s fielding to the bowling of Sharpe is a
treat to witness when the latter is having his day out.
Against the weak Victorian team Sharpe had seemed an
expensive failure, but on New Year’s Day very few of his
thunderbolts went through the field. It is in taking and
returning the fast ones that M‘Gregor shines.
* #.■-#.
At, Ballarat, the other day, a pony named
Masher Boy dropped down dead just after Winning a
race. Pity a few more masher boys don’t follow the
example of the nobler animal.
# # »
Backers started the New Year with old-
fashioned faces at Flemington, for they had a “ had day ”
in spite of fine weather and lively sport, and the genial
absence of His Excellency and suite. Pygmalion was a
great trial to punters, who went down with the deliberate
intention of winning at least one wager on that handsome
animal. The “ glorious uncertainty,” &c., chipped in as
usual. For the Standish Handicap the “ dead bird” was
stopped by a had start, and in the Bagot Stakes race l;c
was pulled about by his jockey (who rode strictly to in-
judicious orders, no doubt) until nobody marvelled at
the horse’s inability to go in and win when the jockev
gave him leave.

tisements with which he was then (and is now) gulling
the public. On his own showing the “reliable turf
adviser,” who had only to back his own prophecies and
acquire boundless wealth, was an utterly impecunious
lodger aftlie'otherwise respectable address whence he
issued his impudent appeals to the ignorant. •

Canny Donald Dinnie has come down to sign-
ing himself “cx-champion athlete of the world,” but
announces that he is still ready to meet any Australian
native in an “ all-round match.” Donald’s idea of an all-
round athlete is the one humorous thing about him. He
isn’t open to run, walk, jump, row, swim, and box any
Australian. He will throw a varied assortment of weights
two inches further than a man who hasn’t devoted his life
to such a dreary business, and then wrestle the novice in
a few particular styles of his own invention. Champions
aa - e tiring people, anyway.
# « #
London Referee says that having seen Sullivan
and all the modern hurricane fighters at their best, there
has been nobody within the last 10 years who “could rival
John Lawrence at the game—not as a sprinter in boxing,
that is. He was tremendously powerful, quick on his
feet, good in the wind, clever, and undeniably vicious.
In the last 20 years I never saw anyone who ought to be
in it with him for half-a-dozen three-minute'glove-fighting
rounds.”
* # #
The committee of N.S.W. National Regatta
firmly refuse to allow coloured sails, on the ground that
such sails would lead to gambling amongst that large
section of the public who now know nothing about boats
and boat-sailing, hut are prepared, if the game is made
intelligible to them, to bet on it just as they would bet on
a horse, or a jumping-frog, or a box of dice.
# * *
It is claimed by the coloured-sail people that, in
the long run, their system is bound to beat the white sail
iii public favour. If that is so, then all the coloured-sail
men need do is go right ahead and wipe the others out,
as the fittest will survive. Apropos, The Bulletin would
suggest that theowners of the boats with gaudy sails should
occasionally wash the ostensibly white area of their sails.
At present, the Flying Yacht Squadron Ltd.’s “white
wings ” look, for the most part, like the dirty, garish
finery of a siren in the cage of the Water Police Court.
* * ~ * N
The colored-sail meh now threaten a regatta,
which promises to be a success.

* # #
“ Nothing was found on the deceased save a
race-card and a ham-sandwich,” says an English paper,
concerning a man who fell down dead on a racecourse.
A race had just finished when he shuffled off, and as there
was a pencil-mark on the dead man's eard against the'
horse that ran fourth,, it was presumed that he had
hacked that animal for everything he had in the world,
barring the ham-sandwich, and then died of frenzy.
* * #
There were some athletic sports recently in
a N.S.W. backblocks settlement, and owing to a number
of scratchings the big foot-race came on with only three
competitors. One of these was the favourite and great
gun of the district, and the other two were common out-
siders—as common as they could be. The prize was £lO
and the unseeing multitude regarded it as a dead cer-
tainty for the favourite. That individual himself regarded
the matter in a totally different light, as he had just been
seized with a violent stomach-ache and couldn’t run a
yard, but he didn’t publish his ailment. Instead, he took
one of the Competitors aside, and pointed out that the
race lay practically between the two of them, the third
man being no good, therefore, the best thing was to
strike an agreement that, whichever won, they should
divide the stakes. Proposal accepted with acclamation.
Then he took the other competitor aside, and pointed'out
that the race practically lay between them, because the
second man was no good, and therefore they had better
divide the stakes in the same manner. This proposition
was also agreed to with internal cheers. By the time the
race started (he favourite’s stomach-ache was so bad that
he could hardly stand, so he only ran about six yards and
then fell. And as he lay in the grass in his misery he
consoled himself with the knowledge that the other two
men were running themselves to death under a blazing
sun for his special benefit, and that no matter how it
ended, he had made a dead certainty of one-half the
money. It was a good and noble device, and some day a
man with a dead horse will win half the Melbourne Cup
prize by just such a scheme.
# # *
O. S. Agnew, of Waverley, Tasmania, who now
owns The Assyrian—the horse that won the Melbourne
Cup in mud up to his saddle girths—has some fine young-
sters coining on, likely to give good accounts of them-
selves. The Assyrian’s stock show better as they mature,
and his three and four-year-olds have come well to the
fore at the Tasmanian Spring Meeting. We would like to
see some of his stock allowed to mature well before being
raced at all. Many of the best English breeders are now
dead against racing mere babies.

performed his task in four.

• * *
Of 11 men capsized out of a boat during the
big squall on Saturday night in Sydney harbour, 10 were
rescued by, their ship’s boat; the eleventh was lost, and
when his body was recovered his right hand had been
bitten off by a shark. Curious that in all the history of
Sydney amateur boatsailing, with its numberless cap-
sizes, there is no authenticated instance of a man having
been snapped up by a shark while swimming or banning
on to his overturned craft.

An ancient prize-fight“ The men then,fought
with their fists and rested, and then fopght and rested
again. Then ‘they wente togedyr by the neckys,’ and
with their teeth tore each other’s leathern coats and the
flesh beneath. The end seemed to have come when
Whithorn cast Fisher on the ground; but in the deadly
wrestle, more by hap than strength, ‘that innocent re-
cover'd upon his kneeys, and took that fals pelcr
(appealer) by the nose with hys tethe and put hys thombe
in hys yee, that the peler cryde owte and p'rayde ,hym of
niarcy, for he was fals unto God and unto hym.’ ' Thus
ended the duel, and the Judge pronounced sentence upon
the approver, whose fate is thus piously recorded—‘ and
therm he was confessyd and hanggyd, of whos soule God.
have marcy. Amen. ’ ” This mayn’thave been according
to Queensherry rules, but it was very straightforward and
had a lot more solid combat in it than the so called “fight”
between the amateur champions of N.S.W. and Banana-
land the other night,

he would not meet him. Jonnel showed , himself too
eager to wriggle out of a match with the best Australian,
as, in ihe.eventof Jackson winning, lie would refuse to
meet Peter under the colour-line, or Slav in as a beaten
man. Slavin, however, has checkmated him by drawing
out of his match with Jackson, and Sullivan will now
have no loop-hole to wriggle out of.

The greatest jokes of this season of jokes are a
certainjockey’s “ineffectual attempts to get up with his
horse on the inside.” The public plank their money
right royally on the animal, and then have to wink the
other eye as they see the rider make his “ineffectual
attempt,to get up on the inside,” and then pull out when
too late to get round his horses. The simple-minded
jocks.! They are expected to pull out and let the great
man through.
# - * *
The writer was one night at Lillie Bridge
(Sydney), sitting next to a youngster, who could not have
been more than ten years of age, when the hopeful,
unasked, tendered the information that he had put“half-
a-caser ” upon a certain pony. The race was run, and
the youngster then stated, with much bad language, that
the pony he had backed had run second, and, he con-
tinued, holding up a bundle wrapped in a grimy hand-
kerchief, ‘-‘l’m holding the blank bloke’s clothes as is
riding the winner, and blime if he didn’t tell me he was
‘stiff.’”

The appointment of Colonel Roberts as
N.S.W. Under-Secretary for Defence is one step
in the right and one step in the wrong direction.
The Colonel lias been one of the N.S.W. military
coterie, and as su’ch can hardly be expected to
shake himself entirely free from its influence.
A reporter attached to the staff of a
“great” journal has just been dismissed for
taking a holy bun-fight as “read”—for, in fact,
writing up a formal notice of the ghastly affair
without taking the trouble to go to the hall and
listen to the dismal cackle ef the clergy interested.
The N.S.W. Single-taxers have now
apparently secured control of the Workman,
organ of the Trades and Labour Council, which is
being edited by Mr. George Black, M.L.A. The
late editor, who seems to have been displaced at
the instance of the Georgite party, ran the paper
on Protectionist lines.
- Probably nothing more pitiable ever
occurred than an incident connected with the
Bank of Y.D.L. smash at Hobart. “Ted”
Wilson, Who was one Of the three' director's,
imagined he was a ruined man, and that nothing
but starvation was in store for him ; though, as a
matter of fact, he was still well off. To try and
undeceive him they got a thousand sovereigns
from a bank, and strewed them all over the
counterpane of his t^ed— his death-bed as it turned
out and let him v sce, touch, and handle them.
It was all no [use, for the mind had gone, and
nothing on earth could convince him he was not a
ruined man.
The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars lias been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and:refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained, i heir name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gm fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A, Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San ‘
Francisco.*

A Plot for a Play.
(Special Bulletin Translation.)
In the smoking-room after dinner, the Hebrew
theatre-manager, Pereira, stood before the fire,
holding in his hand a little glass of curajoa, like an
enormous ruby.
“ The story,” he said, “the whole thing lies in
that. A piece is no good unless you can tell the
story in five minutes. When an author comes to
me at breakfast-time to talk about his comedy, I
stop him at once—‘ Will you be through by the
time I finish this egg? ’ If he can tdo it, there is
nothing in the play.” And Pereira gulped down
his ourajoa. ,
“I’m not a dramatic author,” said Maurice, the
attache, from the depths of the arm-chair in
which he was buried, “ but if you like, Pereira,
I’ll tell you an anecdote out of which I think a
man in that line might make something. But it
takes a very short time to eat an egg, you know.”
“Well, say an omelette,” laughed the Jew.
“ But the ideas of society gentlemen about plots—
I don’t expect much from them. However, go
a h ea( J
“ It is a story that went the round of the salons
when I was in Vienna. There was a doctor there
then with a great reputation for heart-disease; his
name was—say, Arnold. Though hardly 40, he
had a splendid practice. He was a fine, distin-
guished-looking man with regular features, big,
blond Austrian whiskers, and a pair of eyes after
the American style—blue, and cold as steel. A
Russian family staying in the city—let us call
them the Skebeloffs —called the doctor in to
attend to the daughter of the house ; and the
specialist discovered on the first examination the
commencement of an aneurism. It must have
been very embarrassing to sound Miss Macha.
Just think of it, applying his ear to the breast of
a charming blonde of 19, and tapping above her
heart as though to say —may I come in?”
“Now, Maurice,” interrupted the host, no
low-comedy jokes. You promised us a drama.
“You shall have it, make your mind easy.
Finally the doctor fell head over ears in love,
asked for Miss Macha’s hand, was allowed to pay
his addresses, and at the end of three months
married her. The young wife was a great success
in Viennese society. They were an interesting
couple. The doctor loved Macha both as wife
and as patient; he adored her and lavished on her
all his skill. The little romance delighted the
sentimental Teutons. Madame Arnold, whose
health was visibly being re-established, began to
make frequent appearances in society, even danced
occasionally .”
“ In spite, of her heart-disease :
“ Yes; the young woman seemed so far re-
covered that Arnold, as her doctor, allowed her a
waltz, though I think he would have liked, as a
jealous husband, to forbid it. Captain de Blaze-
witz, an Apollo in a white uniform, was always
the first to have his name down on Madame
Arnold’s ball programme, and pressed her ten-
derly to the gold lace on his breast. The old
story of Mars and Venus .”
“Go on, Maurice. Your actors are on the
stage ; let them get to business.”
“ Very well! One day the doctor discovered a
packet of letters .”
“Too stale, the packet of letters!”
“ Pereira, you’re a nuisance ! You can make it
what you like; in my story they are letters.’
“That give the husband proof of his dishonour,
do they not?”
“So it seems.”
“ And put into his mind a project of venge-
ance ?”
• If you know the story, tell it yourself ?”
“ No, my boy, I’m only clearing the ground.
Well, the husband took his revenge ”
“ By one of those crimes that remain for ever
hidden.”
“ Then how did it become known ?”
“ Because the doctor told it. Yes, the culprit
himself, later on, yielding to that irresistible,
fatal impulse to impart his secret, that exists in
all men, and that renders the Catholic confes-
sional one of the must— —”
“Oh ! We know all that !”
“ I won’t say another word,” growled the young
man, sulkily. _ ...
“ Don’t lose your temper,” replied Pereira, with
good-humoured insolence, “we are just saving
you the trouble of finishing your sentences, ihat
is the true theatre style. The husband, then ’
“Resolved on a terrible vengeance, only pos-
sible for a man in his profession. Macha was not
completely cured, as he—the specialist well
knew, of the malady which he had treated for two
years with so much care and tenderness. He set
to work to bring it back again. Restraining his
anger, he carefully adopted towards his wife the
attitude of a restless, suspicious husband, and so
threw her into a state of fear and inquietude.
He knew, by the letters he had discovered, what
an ungovernable passion had taken possession of
the two lovers, and he was certain that, even in
the midst of dangers, they_ would always seek
some way of meeting. Of this he took advantage.
Thenceforth, some mysterious power threw all
sorts of little obstacles between Macha and de
Blazewitz. Without separating them altogether,
it made them miss their appointments, interrupted
their correspondence, disturbed and poisoned
their loves ; and under this life of agitation and
wearing emotion, Madame Arnold’s health
gave way again in an alarming manner.
The doctor was killing his _ wife with the
same certitude and precision with which he had
previously cured her. The hour of wild fear
that gives a feverish activity to the circulation
would, by his consummate skill, be followed up by
long days of sadness, congesting the heart and
holding back the blood; then he would suddenly
appear to throw off his jealousy, and be touched
to tears by the sufferings of his wife ‘ But what
can be the matter, my poor darling ?’ he would
say. ‘My diagnosis is all at fault. You have all
the appearances of a person dying of grief; are
you not happy with me ?’ And so, while gloating
with a diabolical joy over the progress of the
disease, he crucified his victim with his hypo-
critical despair. At the end of six months the
syncopes became more frequent, the palpitations
more rapid; the most alarming symptoms of
aneurism had re-appeared. . . Come, Pereira,
you don’t interrupt me now !”
“ Ah, well, that’s the second act—the point of
the plot. But the denouement, the denouement!”
11.
“ Denouement, sir ? Coming!” cried Maurice,
with the tone of a waiter in a restaurant.
“One night, the doctor burst into his wife’s
room like a tempest: ‘Madame,’he shouted, ‘I
know all. M. de Blazewitz is your lover !’
“Poor Macha grew pale as a sheet, and the
blue shade of death came to her lips.
“ * Kill me !’ she said. It was precisely what
Arnold meant to do.
“‘ I shall not lift a hand against a woman, he
replied. ‘Your accomplice has paid for two. I
have just fought M. de Blazewitz and killed
him!’
“ Maoha dropped lifeless to the floor.
“The doctor had lied. He would not have
dared to touch a hair of the captain, who was
reputed to be the best shot in Vienna.
“Ho knelt beside his wife and took her hand.
The pulse was still beating. He applied remedies
and revived her.
“ ‘Now go and put on a ball-dress, and all your
diamonds,’ he ordered. ‘ You will accompany me
to the French embassy. We have an invitation.’
“ ‘ Never ! I could never do it ! ’
“ ‘You must do it. I made a quarrel at cards
the pretext of my duel with de Blazewitz, but you
are compromised. You must be seen to-night on
my arm ; or it will be believed that I fought about
you, and I shall be dishonoured. Go and dress, I
insist on it.’
“The unhappy woman could only obey. How
was she to resist the man she had so deeply
wronged? She completed her toilet —with what
agony ! —and her husband dragged her to the ball
at the embassy. There, half-dead, she sank down,
rather than sat, in the entrance-hall, where the
footman every minute called the names of the
guests as they arrived. . ,
“ The doctor, in full dress, splendid with all his
decorations, stood behind his wife’s chair.
“Suddenly, after a quick glance in the ante-
chamber, he stooped to Macha’s ear, as if to
whisper a compliment.
“ ‘Your grief did not kill you, then ? ’
“ ‘Not yet, unhappily !’ murmured the tortured
“‘Well, look there,’ he said, pointing to the
door, ‘ and die of joy ! ’
“At that moment the footman, in a sonorous
voice, announced :
“ ‘ Captain Baron de Blazewitz ! ’
“ The handsome officer entered, a smile on his
lips, and his eyes, as usual, sought at once for his
mistress. He barely recognised her.
“ Macha had just started from her seat, as
though moved by a spring. She was livid under
her jewellery. She gave him a wild stare,_ then
lifted her hand to her throat, and fell heavily to
the ground, dead, really dead this time.
“Therewas a terrible uproar. The doctor threw
himself with heartrending cries on his wife’s body,
and the despair of De Blazewitz would have
caused a scandal if a friend had not dragged him
away.
“ The guests all hurried off, the waiters ate the
supper, and the ambassadress was much annoyed,
because she had had some grotesque masks made
expressly for the cotillion, which she had ex-
pected to be a great success.”
111.
When Maurice had finished there was a moment’s
silence. A sort of shiver went round, and even
Pereira did himself injustice by not delivering any
stupid remarks. Just then the hostess lifted the
portiere of the smoking-room.
“Well, gentlemen, have you finished your
cigars ? The ladies are waiting for you. ”
On the way to the salon Pereira took Maurice
by the arm.
“ And what became of the doctor ?”
“ As I said, he almost boasted, in an imprudent
moment, of his crime, which, however, has escaped
all punishment. But he was not able to stay in
Vienna. He is now at Warsaw, where he has a
very large practice, and continues to repeat to
those suffering from his speciality : ‘ Above all,
no emotion, no emotion !’ But what do you think
of my story as a subject for a play ?”
“Impossible, my dear fellow. All the paper?
would say it was copied from Octavo Feuillet'a
‘Julie.’”
FRANCOIS COPPEE.
William Rolleston, member for Christ-
church (M.L.), is an individual who never knows
when to stop talking. There was a political
banquet the other day, in that town of howling
desolation called Balclutha, and one politician
after another jawed in solemn cadences till mid-
night. Then when the guests were partially
drunk, and in the last stage of weariness, Rolle-
ston got up, and from twelve till two he snuffled
along in a grievous voice about the policy of the
Government, and the Bill for the recovery of lost
tailings, and the scheme for compensating miner's
whose crushing-plant had been struck by light-
ning. Most of the guests fell asleep and bobbed
their heads dejectedly up and down; the bass
iron bells of Balclutha struck two with a hollow
boom ; and Vincent Pyke, who wasn’t asleep,
snored with a hideous “ wuff ” and a
“gvvook” and a sound like “howpff,” in th«
hope that Rolleston would take the hint; but he
didn’t. Then Pyke tossed up with his next neigh-
bour which would fall with a crash on the floor
and thus make a diversion, and the next man
lost, and Pyke kicked his chair from under him,
so that he came down with a roar. He hit his
head against something, and got up howling.
“ You old ruffian!” he said briefly to Pyke,
“what did you do that for?” “ Time to go to
bed, old man,” responded Pyke blandly ; “ I feel
that way myself.” “ You feel,” said the victim,
holding his damaged head; “I’ll make you feel,
you miserable old sinner, you— —” Their friends
woke up at this stage and wrenched the com-
batants apart, but Rolleston had lost the thread
of his discourse, and as he couldn’t recover it, the
meeting broke up and stumbled through the
deserted streets on its way home.
Some curious objects have been unloaded
on to the N.S. Wales bench at various times, and
some peculiar decisions have resulted in conse-
quence thereof. Here is a case in point. The
prisoner was being tried for escaping from gaol.
The Crown proved that he had climbed over the
wall, and kept ahead of his pursuers for three
days, and was then captured 60 miles from the
gaol. Then the Judge chipped in and directed an
acquittal. “For,” he said, “the Crown has
proved that the prisoner, after getting out, was,
after three days, captured and brought back.
Therefore, he did not escape for there he is in the
dock.” Wherefore, it follows from this dictum that
no man who is captured can be indicted for this
offence; and if he is not captured he can’t be
indicted. So that on this decision—which dees
not seem to have been followed in later cases—
the crime of escape seems to be about the safest
one in the whole code. If the prisoner is
caught he can’t be convicted, and if he is not
caught he can’t be punished. The Judge who
gave that luminous decision was probably first
cousin to the Kempsey jury which found a
prisoner “ not guilty ” with “ a recommendation
to mercy on account of his excellent character
and more than likely was twin brother to the
Bathurst jury which returned a verdict of
“ guilty ” with a recommendation to mercy, “ on
account of a doubt they had as to whether he was
the man who did it.” .

that retailed by the leading West-end hosiers.

At Large.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
An alarm from the hulks on the river ! a shouting
and baying of hounds,
And the boil shrieking out in a fury that a convict
has broken the bounds.
Ere the gang-, had returned from the quarries,
that in shadows of twilight were draped,
One amongst them, one dare-devil Maurice, had
“ ovalled ” his rings and escaped ;
When the warders, ere daylight was sped, came
to muster their villainous charge,
The intelligence speedily spread that “Two Hun-
dred and Three ” was at large.
Then the stillness on river and shore the alarm-
bell discordantly broke,
And the signal-guns’ rancorous roar ev’ry echo to
action awoke.
Down the stream, thus recalled, came the boats,
with their scowling-eyed, dark-visaged loads,
The b:oad-arrow upon their grey coats, clay-
-1 e (neared from their toil on the roads.
And tne ships, which had lain all deserted, gently
rolled by the river’s light swells,
Hordes invaded, dark-faced and brown-shirted,
who went growling below to their cells.
Ere the last link of daylight had parted, with a
difficult task to discharge,
Armed and wary the warders had started on the
track of the convict at large.
But the trail was not easy to follow, and the
forests were gloomy and grey,
And in ev’ry dark fog-haunted hollow, twenty men
might have hidden away ;
All the marshes were screened in obscurity, and
the reeds by the river grew high,
Where the water-fowl built in security, and the
outcast and hunted might lie.
Haunts no sunlight could ever make cheerful,
where the gleam of the keen wintry moon
Showed the hulks lying, sombre and fearful, in a
crescent of misty lagoon.
So the morning came in with the sun, and the day-
light illumined the marge
Of the swamp where the search had begun, and
“ Two Hundred and Three ” still at large.
Hunted down like some beast of ill-omen that the
trappers were zealous to snare,
Game this badgered and ravenous thing, driven
out of his poisonous lair.
Whilst the vapours rose thickest around him,
rising densely from marshland and fen,
He had stolen forth down to the seaport, to the
houses—-and prisons —of men ;
Through the streets in the silence of midnight,
creeping onward, soiled, tattered and chased,
Did he slink in the shadows of walls, with a guilty
and desperate haste.
And so down to the sea-bitten crags, where the
jaws of great caverns lay wide,
Which the waves, like grim janitors, closed with
the bars and the bolts of the tide.
Here, a week, in the clefts of the rocks, like an
animal, crouching, he lay,
Only treading the sands when the moon, like a
Venus, rose out of the bay;
Here he hungered, afraid of the day, and yet
dreading the phantoms of night,
Like a creature that Heaven had banned, and cast
out of the pale of its light.
Still the search went relentlessly on, for a price
had been set on his head,
And the country was out at his heels, to recapture
him, living or dead ;
Not a nook, not a cranny was there that the
searchers unvisited left,
Till the felon fled forth in the night, of his last
rocky refuge bereft.
’Twas a night when the turbulent wind sent a
voice over mountain and plain,
When the tempest—a madman let loose—came to
smite all the land with its rain ;
When there rested a thunderous frown on the
lowering brows of the sky,
And the heavens were furrowed, as though by
the flash of a passionate eye,
Then this tigerish beast of the chase bent his
head, with a curse, to the blast,
As he doubled and ran like a hare, till pursuers
were distanced at last.
Then the elements wielded the knout in the
powerful hands of the gale,
And they wrung from him groanings of anguish,
as they whipped him with merciless hail;
It was then, when he felt that endurance had
attained to its limits at last,
That he saw a faint pencil of light through the
gloom in a lull of the blast;
And the turn of a slippery road, as the storm fora
time grew less bitter,
Brought him, panting and wet and exhausted, to
the lonely bark-hut of a splitter.
There was hope in his callous despair of the shelter
that Nature denied,
But his hand, raised to knock, was arrested by the
murmur of voices inside.
So he listened, and placing his eye to a knot-hole
that seamed the bark wall,
He beheld to what abyss of want those contrived
in God’s imago may fall.
’Twas a cheerless and comfortless room, scarcely
lit by some smouldering wood ;
In the cupboard, bare shelves for the hungry ; a
few crumbs on the table for food ;
Through the cracks in the roof feU the rain, and
the wind whistled keen as a knife—
The convict looked closer and saw, o’er the coals
crouched, a man and his wife.
“ Hear the wind !” said the man, with a shiver,
“a wintry night, stormy and bleak ;
God help the poor wretch from the hulks, who’s
been wand’ring at large all the week,
If he’s not found a shelter to-night. Ah ! he
can’t be more hopeless than we,
For it’s poverty, wife, on this earth that’s the
fetter of those that are free.
There’s a hundred pound set on his head. Quite
a fortune; God help him !” he sighed.
“Oh, Dan, Dan, if we only could get it!” the
woman, in agony, cried.
“A hundred pounds! Dan, it would save her,
and the little life’s ebbing away,”
And the convict’s eye followed the mother’s to the
bed where a dying child lay.
All the storms that were raging around him waged
Queenslanders must have large heads ! The
celebrated firm of hatters, B. Mountcastle & Sons,
Albert-Street, Brisbane, keep hats in stock up to
size 7f.'

a war in the criminal’s breast,
But the voice of compassion found entry and set
evil for ever at rest;
From its fastenings torn open, the door quickly
yields to a blow from his fist,
And he stands pale and haggard before them,
with the fragments of chain on his wrist.
“ I am he whom the Law sets its price on ! ” this
he cries looking jaded and wild,
“ Come and chain me and claim your reward ; ’tis
my life for the life of your child ! ”
In the morning came troopers, hard riding, to the
splitter’s hut under the hill,
And the convict, some ten days in hiding, was to
herd with the criminals still.
There were few knew the story I’ve told, until
Heath had made out his discharge ;
But the child, who yet lived to grow old, owed her
life to the Convict at Large.
BERNARD ESPINASSE.
Melbourne, 30th November, 1891.
The honours of the entrance examination
at the University of London were carried olf by a
little Scotch lass of 20, Charlotte Higgins, over
1600 male students.
The respective wives of the New York
millionaire Astors are fighting about their titles.
Mrs. William Astor dares dispute with Mrs.
William Waldorf Astor the right to the title of
“Mrs. Astor.” As a consequence William
Waldorf Astor is building a great hotel to shut
out the sun from the home of Mrs. William Astor.
When Waldorf Astor’s hotel is finished William
Astor’s house will be dark and gloomy as a
dungeon.
Teetotaller Cupp, of Auckland, M.L.,
called for three cheers for the Queen at a meeting
there recently. The Cupp that cheers but not
inebriates.
“J. Liddle, solicitor, formerly of Brisbane,”
is advertised for in Lloyds Weekly.” If this be
the well-known solicitor of the good days in Mel-
bourne, it will be found that he died in Northern
Queensland some time ago under very melancholy
circumstances.
Dear Bulletin, — During Sir Geo. Grey’s
Governorship of Maoriland, and at the time th e
war was in progress 'at Korarareka, Bay of
Islands, he was standing on a slight elevation
wa,telling an attack by friendly natives and
colonial troops on a stronghold of the common
enemy. The combined force? succeeded in taking
possession of th s e “ pah,” when a friendly native
chief, standing by Sir George, in his excitement,
cried out “E pai ana.” (It is good.) ' Grey,
whose knowledge of the Maori language was then
only a slight one, asked the native the meaning of
his exclamaflon. On the chief replying “It is
good,” the Governor said, “Yes, I understand
that ‘pai’ is the Ma.ri word for ‘good,’ but
what do the words ‘e’ and ‘ana’ signify?”
This puzzled the chief, but ho eventually gave Grey
to understand that the prefix and suffix had no
significant meaning and were only used to indicate
the tense, as “ I -pai ana ” would read “It was
good.” Sir George explained to the Maori that
this seemed strange to him as every English word
had a meaning of its own, no matter how placed in
a sentence. Presently, the English troops, with
one voice, shouted “ Hip-Hip-Hurrah ! ” The
chief asked the Governor the meaning of the cry,
and he explained it as a shout of victory. Here
tlic native nbn-plussed Grey by saying that he
always understood “ Hurrah ” to be a cry of
victory, but what was the meaning of “ Hip-Hip?”
—Wini Kerki.
Apropos of wrecks in general, here is a
suggestion. Why do not all ocean-going vessels
carry a powerful rocket-apparatus of their own ?
When a wreck takes place, on almost any part of
the coast, there is invariably someone on the shore
to. run about aimlessly, and scream for help, and
this individual could always catch a rope—unless
he is a complete idiot—-when it is fired at him.
But there is.no means of firing a rope at him, and
the rocket apparatus is invariably from 10 to 500
miles away across a bumpy country, and can’t be
got on the scene in time. The one necessity of
the ease is to get the stranded vessel and the
rocket apparatus together ; the apparatus never is
there—the vessel always is, therefore the
apparatus should be on board it. The vessel, in
fact, is the one feature which is never absent at a
wreck, and if the rocket arrangements were fixed
to it they would never be absent either. There is
no going behind this logic.
